Definition of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Definition of knowledge and division into tasawwur and tasdiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq and recall the definition of 'ilm as the form obtained from a thing in the intellect.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either conception or assent; the latter is acceptance of a predicative relation.

This passage introduces the foundational division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq. The commentator clarifies that 'ilm is the intellectual form of a thing, and he explains why the author omitted a full definition. Understanding this division is crucial for later discussions on the types of knowledge in logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division of knowledge
The matn states: 'Knowledge, if it is acceptance of a relation, is assent.' This implies that the other branch is conception. Find the two branches.
The matn does not explicitly name tasawwur, but the division is clear from the conditional.
2
Define 'ilm from the commentary
The commentary defines 'ilm as 'al-sura al-hasila min al-shay’i ‘inda al-‘aql' (the form obtained from the thing in the intellect). This is the definition you must memorize.
The author himself did not define it, but the commentator provides the definition.
3
Understand 'muqaddima'
The commentary expands on what an introduction is: both in words (alfaz) and meanings (ma'ani). It is a preliminary body of knowledge that clarifies three things: the definition of logic, the need for it, and its subject.
The term is taken from 'al-jaysh' (army), indicating a preliminary force.
4
Clarify the predicate relation
'Idh'an' is defined as belief in a predicative affirmative relation (al-nisba al-khabariyya al-thubutiyya). This is what distinguishes tasdiq from mere conceptualization.
A predicative affirmative relation is the connection between subject and predicate in a positive statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة: العلم ان كان اذعاناً للنسبة فتصديق قوله «مقدمة»: اى هذه مقدمة يتبين فيها امور ثلاثة رسم المنطق و بيان الحاجة اليه و موضوعه وهى مأخوذة الجيش و المراد منها هيهنا ان كان الكتاب عبارة عن الالفاظ و العبارات طائفة من الكلام قدمت امام المقصود لارتباط المقصود بها و نفعها فيه وان كان عبارة عن المعانى فالمراد من المقدمة طائفة من المعاني يوجب الاطلاع عليها بصيرة فى الشروع وتجويز الاحتمالات الاخر في الكتاب يستدعى جوازها فى المقدمة التي هي جزئه لكن القوم لم یزیدوا على الالفاظ والمعانى فى هذا الباب شيئاً. قوله «العلم»: هو الصورة الحاصلة من الشيء عند العقل و المصنف لم يتعرض بتعريفه اما لكفاية التصور بوجه ما فى مقام التقسيم واما لان تعريف العلم مشهور مستفيض و اما لان العلم بديهى التصور على ما قيل. قوله ان كان اذعاناً »: اى اعتقاداً بالنسبة الخبرية الثبوتية
Introduction: Knowledge, if it is acceptance of a relation, is assent. His saying 'introduction': i.e., this is an introduction in which three matters are clarified: the definition of logic, the explanation of the need for it, and its subject matter. It is taken from the army. What is intended by it here: if the book consists of words and expressions, then it is a portion of speech placed before the intended [subject] because the intended [subject] is connected to it and it is beneficial for it; and if it consists of meanings, then the intended meaning of the introduction is a portion of meanings, acquaintance with which necessitates insight in the beginning and permission of other possibilities in the book; it requires their permissibility in the introduction which is part of it. But the people (scholars) did not add anything beyond words and meanings in this regard. His saying 'knowledge': it is the form obtained from the thing in the intellect. The author did not attempt to define it; either because a certain conceptualization is sufficient in the context of division, or because the definition of knowledge is well-known and widespread, or because knowledge is axiomatic in conceptualization as has been said. His saying 'if it is acceptance': i.e., belief in the predicative affirmative relation.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage defines the foundational division of knowledge (al-'ilm) into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The matn states: 'Knowledge, if it is acceptance of a relation, is assent.' The commentary then explains that 'mukaddima' (introduction) here clarifies three things: the definition of logic, the need for it, and its subject. The commentator notes that 'mukaddima' is taken from ‘al-jaysh’ (army), meaning a preliminary body of knowledge. The intended meaning depends on whether the book is considered as words/expressions (alfaz) or meanings (ma‘ani). For words, it is a portion of speech placed before the main text. For meanings, it is a set of meanings that, when known, provide insight and allow for other possibilities in the book. The author then defines 'al-ilm' as the form of a thing in the intellect. The commentator offers reasons why the author did not define 'ilm' explicitly: either because a general concept is sufficient for division, or because the definition is famous, or because 'ilm' is intuitively clear. Finally, 'idh‘an' (acceptance) is explained as belief in a predicative affirmative relation (al-nisba al-khabariyya al-thubutiyya).

تصديق
اذعان
مقدمة
النسبة الخبرية الثبوتية

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the definition of 'al-ilm'?
A) Belief in a predicative relation.
B) The form obtained from a thing in the intellect.
C) The introduction to logic.
D) Acceptance of a proposition.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is either a mental form (tasawwur) or an assent (tasdiq) to a predicative relation, with the latter involving acceptance (idh'an).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following as tasawwur or tasdiq: 'The world is temporally originated.' Explain your reasoning.
?

Subject of a Science and Essential Accidents
Author defines subject of science and essential accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') of a given Islamic science and provide an example of an essential accident ('arad dhati) that attaches to it.

Core Idea
The subject of a science defines its scope; essential accidents are properties that flow from its essence.

Every science has a subject matter (mawdu') in which it investigates essential accidents. Logic is no exception. Before diving into the tools of definition and proof, the author clarifies what a 'subject of a science' means and what qualifies as an essential accident. Understanding this will help you see how logic organizes its own inquiry and how you can apply this concept in other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of 'subject of a science'
Find the phrase 'موضوع العلم' in the Arabic passage. The author defines it as that in which a science investigates its essential accidents. Note that this is a general definition applicable to any science.
2
Understand 'essential accident'
Identify the two types of essential accident: primary (like wonder attaching to human) and secondary through a coextensive term (like laughter, which attaches to wonder then metaphorically to human). The key is that the accident inheres in the subject itself.
This distinction helps avoid confusing accidental properties with essential ones.
3
Apply the concept to logic
The subject of logic is the known (ma'lum) insofar as it leads to the unknown. The essential accidents of this subject would be properties like 'being a definition' or 'being a proof' – the very things the passage goes on to explain.
Notice how the passage builds from general principle to specific application.
4
Extend to another Islamic science
Think of another science, like Kalam (theology). Its subject is the Necessary Existent. An essential accident of that subject might be 'existence' or 'oneness'. The requirement is that the accident necessarily attaches to the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهيهنا علم امران من الامور الثلاثة التي وضعت المقدمة لبيانها بقى الكلام فى الامر الثالث و هو تحقيق ان موضوع المنطق ماذا فأشار اليه بقوله وموضوعه الخ. قوله وموضوعه: موضوع العلم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية. و العرض الذاتى ما يعرض الشيء اما اولا و بالذات كالتعجب اللاحق للانسان من حيث انه انسان و اما بواسطة امر مساو لذلك الشيء كالضحك الذى يعرض حقيقة للتعجب ثم ينسب عروضه الى الانسان بالعرض والمجاز فافهم. قوله المعلوم التصورى: اعلم ان موضوع المنطق هو المعرف و الحجة اما المعرف فهو عبارة عن المعلوم التصورى و لكن لا مطلقا بل من حيث انه يوصل الى المجهول التصورى كالحيوان الناطق الموصل الى تصور الانسان. و اما المعلوم التصوري الذى لا يوصل الى المجهول التصورى فلايسمى معرفاً و المنطق لا يبحث عنه كالامور الجزئية المعلومة نحو زيد و عمرو. و اما الحجة فهى عبارة عن المعلوم التصديقى لكن لا مطلقا ايضاً بل من حيث انه يوصل الى المجهول التصديق كقولنا العالم متغير و كل متغير حادث الموصل الى التصديق بقولنا العالم حادث. و اما ما لا يوصل كقولنا النار حارة مثلا فليس بحجة والمنطقى لا ينظر فيه بل يبحث عن المعرف و الحجة من حيث انهما كيف ينبغي ان يترتبا حتى يوصلا الى المجهول. قوله معرفاً: لانه يعرف و يبين حال المجهول التصوري. قوله حجة: لانها تصير سبباً للغلبة على الخصم والحجة في اللغة الغلبة، فهذا من قبيل تسمية السبب باسم المسبب.
Now two of the three matters that the premise was set to explain are known; there remains discussion of the third matter, which is the verification of what the subject matter of logic is. So he pointed to it by his saying: 'and its subject' etc. His saying 'and its subject': the subject of a science is that in which it investigates its essential accidents. And the essential accident is that which attaches to a thing either primarily and essentially, like wonder attaching to a human insofar as he is human, or through something coextensive with that thing, like laughter which attaches in reality to wonder, then its attachment is attributed to the human secondarily and metaphorically, so understand. His saying 'the conceptual known': know that the subject matter of logic is the definition (mu'arraf) and the proof (hujja). As for the definition, it is the conceptual known, but not absolutely, rather insofar as it leads to the conceptual unknown, like 'rational animal' leading to the concept of 'human'. And as for the conceptual known that does not lead to the conceptual unknown, it is not called a definition, and logic does not investigate it, like particular known things such as Zayd and 'Amr. And as for the proof, it is the assented known, but not absolutely either, rather insofar as it leads to the assented unknown, like our saying 'the world is changing and everything changing is originated' leading to the assent to our saying 'the world is originated'. And as for what does not lead, like our saying 'fire is hot' for example, it is not a proof, and the logician does not consider it; rather he investigates the definition and the proof in terms of how they should be arranged so that they lead to the unknown. His saying 'definition': because it defines and clarifies the state of the conceptual unknown. His saying 'proof': because it becomes a cause of victory over the opponent, and 'hujja' in language means victory, so this is a case of naming the cause by the name of the effect.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of logic. The author first notes that two of the three matters from the previous introduction are known, and now the third matter is the subject of logic. He then explains the term 'subject' (mawdu'): the subject of a science is that in which it investigates essential accidents ('arad dhati). He defines essential accident as either primary (like wonder for humans) or secondary through a coextensive term (like laughter, which is really an accident of wonder but attributed to humans metaphorically). Next, he clarifies that the subject of logic is two things: the definition (mu'arraf) and the proof (hujja). The definition is the conceptual known (ma'lum tasawwuri) that leads to a conceptual unknown (majhul tasawwuri), e.g., 'rational animal' leads to the concept 'human'. Particular conceptual knowns like 'Zayd' are not studied because they do not lead to an unknown. The proof is the assented known (ma'lum tasdiqi) that leads to an assented unknown, e.g., the syllogism 'the world is changing; everything changing is originated' leads to the assent that the world is originated. Assertions like 'fire is hot' are not proofs because they are already known. Finally, the author gives the linguistic origin of the terms: 'mu'arraf' comes from 'ta'rif' (to define), and 'hujja' comes from 'ghalaba' (to overcome), indicating that a proof is a means of overcoming an opponent, naming the cause (the argument) by the effect (victory).

Key Terms
موضوع العلم
العرض الذاتي
التعجب
الضحك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, an essential accident can attach to a thing in two ways. What is one example given for the second way (attachment through something coextensive)?
A) Wonder attaching to humans
B) Laughter attaching to humans
C) Change attaching to the world
D) Rationality attaching to animals

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science is defined by the essential accidents it investigates; essential accidents are those that inhere directly or via a coextensive property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the subject of the Islamic science of Tafsir. What is its subject? Give one example of an essential accident that the science investigates.
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, the subject is the act of the legally responsible person (mukallaf). Find an example of an essential accident that is studied in usul al-fiqh (e.g., obligation, prohibition) and explain why it is considered essential.

The Two Tools of Logic: Definition and Proof
Author explains definition and proof as the two tools of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a definition (mu'arraf) and a proof (hujja) by identifying whether a given known leads to a conceptual or an assented unknown.

Core Idea
Logic studies only those knowns that lead to an unknown; definition leads to conceptual unknown, proof leads to assented unknown.

Now that the general subject of logic is established, the author specifies the two types of knowns that are the focus of logic: definition (mu'arraf) and proof (hujja). Both are 'useful' knowns that guide us from what we know to what we do not yet know. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying logical tools correctly in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of known
The author divides knowns into conceptual (tasawwuri) and assented (tasdiqi). Definitions are conceptual knowns that lead to conceptual unknowns; proofs are assented knowns that lead to assented unknowns.
Remember: 'known' here is not any piece of information, but specifically those that function as means to something unknown.
2
Understand what makes a known a 'definition'
A definition (mu'arraf) is a conceptual known that leads to a conceptual unknown. Example: 'rational animal' leads to the concept 'human'. Not all conceptual knowns are definitions – particular concepts like 'Zayd' do not lead to a new general concept.
Logic only studies definitions that are universal and explanatory.
3
Understand what makes a known a 'proof'
A proof (hujja) is an assented known that leads to an assented unknown. Example: the syllogism 'the world is changing; everything changing is originated' leads to the assent that the world is originated. Simple known facts like 'fire is hot' are not proofs because they do not produce new knowledge.
The term 'hujja' linguistically means 'victory', indicating its role in argumentation.
4
Distinguish examples from each category
Given a statement, decide whether it is a definition (conceptual) or proof (assented). For instance, 'human is rational animal' is a definition; 'the world is originated because it changes' is a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Classifying Expressions: Complete vs Incomplete
Classification of expressions into complete and incomplete with subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an Arabic expression is complete or incomplete, and classify a complete expression as informative or performative.

Core Idea
An expression's completeness is defined by whether it can stand alone as a meaningful statement.

In Islamic logic, expressions are first classified by their ability to convey a complete thought. This binary classification is foundational for understanding propositions (qadāyā) and is used across disciplines such as usul al-fiqh and kalam. This lesson unpacks the criteria for completeness and the subtypes that follow.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the criterion for completeness
An expression is complete (tāmm) if it is valid for the speaker to pause (sukūt) after uttering it without needing additional words to convey a full meaning. Example: 'Zayd is standing' is complete.
In Arabic, the test is 'yasihhu al-sukūt 'alayhi'.
2
Classify complete expressions as khabar or inshā'
If the complete expression can be true or false, it is informative (khabar); if it does not admit truth values (e.g., commands, questions), it is performative (inshā').
Khabar is the main type used in logical propositions.
3
Identify incomplete expressions
An expression that cannot stand alone is incomplete (nāqis). For example, 'Zayd's slave' requires more context to convey a complete thought.
Incomplete expressions are not full statements.
4
Subclassify incomplete expressions
If the second part restricts the first, it is restrictive (taqyīdī), e.g., 'virtuous man'. If it does not restrict, it is other (ghayruhu), e.g., 'fifteen'.
This subdivision aids in analyzing compound phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اما تام اى يصح السكوت عليه (٤٣) كـ «زيد قائم».
قوله خبر ان احتمل الصدق والكذب (٤٤) اى من شأنه ان يتصف بهما (٤٥) بان يقال له : صادق او كاذب
قوله «اوانشاء»: ان لم يحتملها . (٤٦) قوله « واما ناقص ان لم يصح السكوت عليه .
قوله «تقييدى»: ان كان الجزء الثاني قيداً للأول (٤٧) نحو: «غلام «زید» و رجل فاضل و «قائم في الدار»
قوله او غيره ان لم يكن الثاني قيداً للاول (٤٩) نحو («في الدار» و «خمسة عشر (۵۰)
قوله و الا فمفرد» اى و ان لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء المعني.
(٥) قوله :والا ففرد: اى و ان لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه فهو مفرد. و هذا النقي وهو قولنا و أن لم يقصد الخ- ينحل الى امور اربعة:
- مالاجزء للفظه لبساطته كهمزة الاستفهام مثلاً فهذا يصدق فيه أنه لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة ولو لانتفاء موضوع تجزء اللفظ.
٢- مالاجزء لمعناه لبساطته ايضاً نحو لفظ الله بالنسبة الى واجب الوجود فانه تصدق فيه القضية السالبة السابقة الذكر ولو لانتفاء موضوع تجزء المعنى.
- ما لا دلالة الجزء لفظه على جزء معناه نحو زيد وعبدالله علماً لشخص فان الاعلام تعتبر قطعاً غير قابلة التجزء في اسمائها و في مسمياتها وان كانت فى الأصل مركبات كعبد الله و محمد على و تأبط شراً و يعليك
-٤- ما يقبل ان يدل جزء لفظه على جزء معناه ولكن لم يقصد ذلك كالحيوان الناطق شعاراً وعلماً الشخص الانسان فان الحيوان الناطق حيث يطلق في جواب السؤال عن زيد وعن عمرو وعن غير هما انها يقصد به ان زيداً انسان فكما يعتبر الجواب بالانسان عن السؤال يزيد قطعة واحدة لا تتجزأ فكذلك ما هو
His statement 'as for complete': i.e., it is valid to pause on it, like 'Zayd is standing'. His statement 'informative': if it admits truth and falsehood; i.e., it is in its nature to be described by them, in that it can be called true or false. His statement 'or performative': if it does not admit them. His statement 'and as for incomplete': if it is not valid to pause on it. His statement 'restrictive': if the second part is a restriction of the first, like 'Zayd's slave', 'virtuous man', and 'standing in the house'. His statement 'or other': if the second is not a restriction of the first, like 'in the house' and 'fifteen'. His statement 'otherwise it is simple': i.e., if it is not intended that a part of it indicates a part of the meaning. His statement 'otherwise it is simple': i.e., if it is not intended that a part of it indicates a part of its meaning, then it is simple. And this negation, which is our statement 'and if it is not intended etc.', resolves into four matters: 
1. What has no part of its wording due to its simplicity, like the interrogative hamza; so it is true to say that no part of it is intended to indicate, even due to the absence of a subject for segmentation. 
2. What has no part of its meaning due to its simplicity also, like the word 'Allah' with respect to the Necessary Existent; so the previous negative proposition is true of it, even due to the absence of a subject for segmentation of meaning. 
3. What has no indication of a part of its wording for a part of its meaning, like 'Zayd' and 'Abdallah' as proper names for a person; for proper names are considered absolutely as not divisible in their names and in their named objects, even if originally they were compounds like 'Abdallah', 'Muhammad Ali', 'Ta'abbata Sharran', and 'Ya'lik'. 
4. What admits that a part of its wording indicates a part of its meaning but this indication is not intended, like 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a title and proper name for the human individual; for when 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' is uttered in answer to the question about Zayd, Amr, and others, it is intended by it that Zayd is a human being; just as the answer with 'human' to the question about Zayd is considered one indivisible piece, so is what is above.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage classifies expressions into complete (tāmm) and incomplete (nāqis). A complete expression is one that can stand alone as a statement, like 'Zayd is standing'. Within complete, there are two types: informative (khabar), which admits truth or falsehood (e.g., a declaration that can be verified or falsified), and performative (inshā'), which does not admit truth or falsehood (e.g., commands, questions). An incomplete expression is one that cannot stand alone; it is further divided into restrictive (taqyīdī), where the second part qualifies the first (e.g., 'Zayd's slave', 'virtuous man'), and other (ghayr), where the second part does not restrict the first (e.g., 'in the house', 'fifteen'). Finally, the phrase 'otherwise it is simple' refers back to the earlier definition of simple (mufrad), and the commentator explains the four types of simple again as a negation of the conditions for compound. This repetition reinforces that any expression that does not have an intended part-for-part indication is considered simple, even if it appears compound. The examples for proper names (like 'Abdallah') and the use of 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a proper name illustrate the nuanced classification.

When Is a Multivord Expression Simple? The Four Cases
Expansion on the definition of simple (mufrad) with four illustrative cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze whether a multivord expression should be treated as simple (mufrad) or compound based on the four conditions listed by the commentator.

Core Idea
An expression is simple if it lacks part-for-part indication, even if it appears to have multiple words.

The commentator expands on the definition of simple (mufrad) by listing four cases where an expression, though having multiple words, is considered simple. This nuanced classification prevents errors in logical analysis, especially when dealing with proper names and technical terms. Understanding these cases sharpens your ability to distinguish between genuine compounds and apparent compounds that function as simple units.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for compound
An expression is compound (murakkab) if its parts are intended to indicate parts of the meaning. For example, 'Zayd's slave' is compound because 'Zayd' indicates the owner and 'slave' indicates the owned.
Intentionality is key.
2
Identify the negation for simple
If the condition for compound is not met, the expression is simple. The commentator explains that this negation resolves into four distinct cases, each showing why part-for-part indication fails.
These cases are mutually exclusive.
3
Apply the four cases to examples
Case 1: No parts in wording (e.g., hamza). Case 2: No parts in meaning (e.g., 'Allah'). Case 3: Parts exist but no indication of parts (e.g., proper name 'Abdallah'). Case 4: Indication exists but not intended (e.g., 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' as a title).
Note the overlap with compound forms in language.
4
Test with new examples
Consider 'تأبط شراً' – it is a proper name, so it falls under case 3. Even though it has words, as a name it is considered simple.
Context determines whether indication is intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله او غيره ان لم يكن الثاني قيداً للاول (٤٩) نحو («في الدار» و «خمسة عشر (۵۰)
قوله و الا فمفرد» اى و ان لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء المعني.

The Tripartite Division of Words into Kalima, Ism, and Adah
Yazdi defines the three word categories in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish the three logical categories of singular words (kalima, ism, adah) based on independence of indication and time indication.

Core Idea
Words are divided by how they indicate meaning: independent with time = verb, independent without time = noun, dependent = particle.

In Islamic logic, words are not just grammatical parts of speech; they are analyzed for how they convey meaning. This lesson introduces the foundational tripartite division of singular terms into kalima (verb), ism (noun), and adah (particle). The distinction hinges on two criteria: whether the word independently indicates its meaning, and whether it indicates time by its form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the passage, note that the author defines kalima, ism, and adah based on the combination of independence (istiqlal) and time indication (dallalah 'ala zaman).
2
Understand independence
Independence means the word can convey its meaning without needing a supplement. Contrast this with particles (adawat) which require other words to complete meaning.
3
Understand time indication
Only kalima (verbs) indicate one of the three times (past, present, future) by their form (hay'ah). Ism does not, and adah is not independent.
4
Apply the definitions to examples
Given a word, determine if it is independent; if yes, check if it indicates time by form → kalima, else ism. If not independent, adah.
The passage gives the example 'nasara' as a kalima because its form always conveys past tense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He [the author] said: 'And that is that it is independent [in indication] — then with indication by its form on one of the three times, it is a kalima; without it, it is an ism; and if not, it is an adah; and likewise.' His statement: 'and that is that it is independent' — meaning in indicating its meaning such that it does not need the addition of a supplement. His statement: 'by its form' — such that whenever its compositional form is realized within a subject matter that is flexible, one of the three times is understood. For example, the form 'nasara' which is composed of three letters, all open, successive — whenever it is realized, the past tense is understood, but on condition that its realization be within a subject matter that is flexible. Thus the objection is not raised by examples like 'jasq' and 'hajar'. His statement: 'kalima' — in the terminology of the logicians, and in the custom of grammarians it is 'verb'. His statement: 'otherwise' — meaning that if it is not independent in indication. 'Adah' — in the terminology of the logicians and is a 'particle' according to grammarians. His statement: 'and likewise' — it is a direct object of an omitted verb, meaning 'add similarly', i.e., as a return, and it contains an indication that this division too is for the absolute singular term, not for the noun alone. And there is a discussion here: it would require that the verb and the particle, if they have unified meaning, be included in 'proper noun' or 'mutawati' or 'mushakkik', even though they are not called by these names; rather it is established in its place that their meanings are not characterized by universality and particularity, so reflect on it. (Reference to Taqrib p. 2120) (5) The author's statement: 'It might be imagined that this applies also to the verbs of approximation (af'al al-muqaraba) based on what is famous among them that they are stripped of indication of time, even though they are not called by that name by anyone, and it does not apply to active and passive participles because they indicate one of the times as well, even though they are nouns by agreement, so the definition fails in both inclusion and exclusion. The answer is that the consideration in indicating one of the times in the definition of the verb and its absence in the definition of the noun is what is established by the original convention. There is no doubt that active and passive participles do not indicate time according to it, and the verbs of approximation indicate it as an acquisition, so there is no problem. From this it becomes clear that if they had not explicitly stated that the defective verbs are particles according to them, it could be said that they enter into the definition of kalima and are instances of it, based on their being independent in indicating the event and one of the times according to the original convention. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the definition of the three types of words: kalima, ism, and adah. The author first states the definition: a word that independently indicates a time by its form is a kalima (verb); if it indicates its meaning without indicating a time, it is an ism (noun); and if it is not independent in indication, it is an adah (particle). The commentator then breaks down each part of the definition. 'Independence' means the word does not require a supplemental element to convey its meaning. 'By its form' refers to the compositional structure that, when realized in a flexible material (like a verb root), conveys a specific time (past, present, future). The example 'nasara' (he helped) is given: its form always indicates the past tense. Conditions are added to exclude non-flexible words like 'jasq' or 'hajar' (proper nouns) that also have a form but do not indicate time. The commentator then clarifies terminology: in logic, 'kalima' means verb, while in grammar it is called 'fi'l'; 'adah' in logic is a particle, corresponding to 'harf' in grammar. Next, the word 'wa-aydan' (and likewise) is explained as indicating that this threefold division applies to all singular terms, not only nouns. A critical discussion follows: if verbs and particles had a single meaning, they would fall under the division of proper noun (alam), univocal (mutawati), or gradational (mushakkik) — but logicians do not classify them as such because their meanings are not universal or particular in the same way. Finally, a common objection is addressed: verbs of approximation (af'al al-muqaraba, e.g., 'kada' 'to be about to') appear to strip away time indication, and active/passive participles indicate time yet are nouns. The answer is that the criterion is the original convention (al-wad' al-awwal), not derived usage. Participles do not primarily indicate time, and verbs of approximation acquire time indication secondarily. Thus the definition holds. The commentator adds that if not for the grammarians' classification of defective verbs as particles, they might fit the definition of kalima based on original convention.

Key Terms
كلمة
اسم
أداة
استقلال
هيئة

A) It has a form composed of letters.
B) It independently indicates a meaning but not a time.
C) It requires a supplement to indicate its meaning.
D) It always has declension.

Key Takeaway
The logical classification of singular words into kalima, ism, and adah depends on independence of indication and, for independent words, whether their form conveys a specific time.

Resolving an Objection: Verbs of Approximation and Participles
Yazdi addresses a potential counterexample to the word classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the logical definitions of kalima and ism against the objection that verbs of approximation (e.g., 'kada') seem not to indicate time and that participles seem to indicate time while being nouns.

Core Idea
Time indication is judged by original convention, not derived usage — thus the definition holds.

The strict logical definitions face a challenge: verbs of approximation (af'al al-muqaraba like 'kada' meaning 'to be about to') appear to have lost their time indication, and active/passive participles (ism fa'il, ism maf'ul) seem to indicate time yet are nouns. The commentator resolves this by distinguishing between original and acquired indication.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Some might think the definition of kalima fails because verbs of approximation are stripped of time indication. Similarly, participles indicate time but are nouns.
The passage calls this a 'wahm' (imagination) but treats it as a serious potential counterexample.
2
Identify the author's solution
The passage says the criterion is the original convention (al-wad' al-awwal). Participles do not indicate time in their original convention; verbs of approximation do indicate time in their original convention.
3
Apply the solution to an example
For a word like 'dārib' (hitter), check its original convention: it primarily indicates the doer, not time. So it remains an ism. For 'kada', its original convention includes time, so it remains a kalima.
4
Reflect on a hypothetical
The commentator adds that if grammarians had not classified defective verbs as particles, they could be considered kalima based on original convention.
This shows the solution is consistent: original convention is the decisive factor.

He [the author] said: 'And that is that it is independent [in indication] — then with indication by its form on one of the three times, it is a kalima; without it, it is an ism; and if not, it is an adah; and likewise.' His statement: 'and that is that it is independent' — meaning in indicating its meaning such that it does not need the addition of a supplement. His statement: 'by its form' — such that whenever its compositional form is realized within a subject matter that is flexible, one of the three times is understood. For example, the form 'nasara' which is composed of three letters, all open, successive — whenever it is realized, the past tense is understood, but on condition that its realization be within a subject matter that is flexible. Thus the objection is not raised by examples like 'jasq' and 'hajar'. His statement: 'kalima' — in the terminology of the logicians, and in the custom of grammarians it is 'verb'. His statement: 'otherwise' — meaning that if it is not independent in indication. 'Adah' — in the terminology of the logicians and is a 'particle' according to grammarians. His statement: 'and likewise' — it is a direct object of an omitted verb, meaning 'add similarly', i.e., as a return, and it contains an indication that this division too is for the absolute singular term, not for the noun alone. And there is a discussion here: it would require that the verb and the particle, if they have unified meaning, be included in 'proper noun' or 'mutawati' or 'mushakkik', even though they are not called by these names; rather it is established in its place that their meanings are not characterized by universality and particularity, so reflect on it. (Reference to Taqrib p. 2120) (5) The author's statement: 'It might be imagined that this applies also to the verbs of approximation (af'al al-muqaraba) based on what is famous among them that they are stripped of indication of time, even though they are not called by that name by anyone, and it does not apply to active and passive participles because they indicate one of the times as well, even though they are nouns by agreement, so the definition fails in both inclusion and exclusion. The answer is that the consideration in indicating one of the times in the definition of the verb and its absence in the definition of the noun is what is established by the original convention. There is no doubt that active and passive participles do not indicate time according to it, and the verbs of approximation indicate it as an acquisition, so there is no problem. From this it becomes clear that if they had not explicitly stated that the defective verbs are particles according to them, it could be said that they enter into the definition of kalima and are instances of it, based on their being independent in indicating the event and one of the times according to the original convention. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the definition of the three types of words: kalima, ism, and adah. The author first states the definition: a word that independently indicates a time by its form is a kalima (verb); if it indicates its meaning without indicating a time, it is an ism (noun); and if it is not independent in indication, it is an adah (particle). The commentator then breaks down each part of the definition. 'Independence' means the word does not require a supplemental element to convey its meaning. 'By its form' refers to the compositional structure that, when realized in a flexible material (like a verb root), conveys a specific time (past, present, future). The example 'nasara' (he helped) is given: its form always indicates the past tense. Conditions are added to exclude non-flexible words like 'jasq' or 'hajar' (proper nouns) that also have a form but do not indicate time. The commentator then clarifies terminology: in logic, 'kalima' means verb, while in grammar it is called 'fi'l'; 'adah' in logic is a particle, corresponding to 'harf' in grammar. Next, the word 'wa-aydan' (and likewise) is explained as indicating that this threefold division applies to all singular terms, not only nouns. A critical discussion follows: if verbs and particles had a single meaning, they would fall under the division of proper noun (alam), univocal (mutawati), or gradational (mushakkik) — but logicians do not classify them as such because their meanings are not universal or particular in the same way. Finally, a common objection is addressed: verbs of approximation (af'al al-muqaraba, e.g., 'kada' 'to be about to') appear to strip away time indication, and active/passive participles indicate time yet are nouns. The answer is that the criterion is the original convention (al-wad' al-awwal), not derived usage. Participles do not primarily indicate time, and verbs of approximation acquire time indication secondarily. Thus the definition holds. The commentator adds that if not for the grammarians' classification of defective verbs as particles, they might fit the definition of kalima based on original convention.

The Three Types of Singular Terms
Yazdi explains the division of singular terms based on unity of meaning and specification by convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish 'alam, mutawati, and mushakkik terms based on their definitions and conditions.

Core Idea
Singular terms are divided by whether meaning is specified and how individuals relate to it.

In this passage, Yazdi presents the foundational division of singular terms in logic. Understanding these three types—proper noun, univocal, and gradational—is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the matn: if meaning is one, then with specification by convention -> 'alam; without specification, if equal -> mutawati; if differing in priority -> mushakkik.
2
Understand 'alam
A proper noun whose meaning is a particular individual by original convention, not just in usage. Example: 'Zayd'.
Specification must be from wad', not isti'mal.
3
Understand mutawati
A univocal term: the universal meaning applies equally to all individuals. Example: 'human' applies to all humans equally.
4
Understand mushakkik
A gradational term: the meaning applies with priority or superiority (shiddah/da'f). Example: 'existence' applies primarily to God, secondarily to creatures.
Variations can be in intensity or priority.
5
Compare with examples
The commentary gives examples: existence is mushakkik, whiteness is mushakkik if pure white is considered more intense. Mutawati terms are like 'human'.
Practice classifying terms.

On the singular and compound and their divisions. If its meaning is one, then with its specification by convention it is a proper noun; without specification, if its individuals are equal it is mutawati; and if they differ in priority or superiority it is mushakkik. His statement: 'if its meaning is one' — meaning: its meaning is one. His statement: 'with its specification' — meaning: its particularity. His statement: 'by convention' — meaning: according to the convention, not usage; for what has a universal denotation in the original convention but is specified in usage, like demonstratives according to the author, is not called a proper noun. And there is a discussion here: what is meant by 'meaning' in this division is either the actual object of the convention or what the word is used for, whether the word is conventionally established for it really or metaphorically. On the first, reality and metaphor are not among the divisions of multiple meaning; on the second, demonstratives according to the author enter into multiple meaning and depart from single meaning, so there is no need to exclude them by the restriction 'by convention'. His statement: 'if equal' — meaning that the truth of this universal meaning upon those individuals is equal. His statement: 'if they differ' — meaning that the truth of this concept upon some individuals is prior ... (then additional commentary from Taqrib) ... (0) His statement: 'by convention' — meaning that specification comes to the meaning from the side of convention, not from usage. For what is conventionally established with a general convention and a general object but is specified in usage, like demonstratives according to the author where he claims that the word 'this' for example is conventionally established for the universal of the present near masculine noun, even though in usage it is only said to a specific person, it is not called a proper noun. (Taqrib p. 22) (ه) His statement: 'or priority' — like the variations that exist among individuals of one reality in intensity and weakness and increase.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage explains the division of singular terms (al-mufrad) into three types: proper noun ('alam), univocal (mutawati), and gradational (mushakkik). The matn states: if the meaning of a singular term is one, and it is specified by convention (i.e., its meaning is a particular individual by original convention), it is a proper noun. If it is not specified, then it is either mutawati if all its individuals equally fall under it, or mushakkik if some are prior or stronger in applying the term. The commentator expands: 'its meaning is one' means the term has a single meaning. 'Specification' means particularity (individuality). 'By convention' indicates that specification comes from the original assignment, not from usage. For example, demonstratives like 'this' have a universal meaning in the original convention but are specified in usage; according to the author, they are not proper nouns. A discussion follows: what is meant by 'meaning'? It could be the actual object of the convention (what the word is literally assigned to) or what the word is used for (including metaphorical usage). If it is the former, then literal and metaphorical uses are not part of the division of multiple meaning; if the latter, demonstratives would be considered words with multiple meanings (since their intended reference is particular but their conventional meaning is universal) and thus would need to be excluded, but the matn's restriction 'by convention' already excludes them. The commentator then clarifies the conditions for mutawati and mushakkik, providing examples: existence is mushakkik because it applies to God primarily and to creatures secondarily; whiteness is mushakkik because pure white is more intense than mixed white. Mutawati terms apply equally, like 'human' to all humans.

متواطئ
مشكك

Singular terms are divided into 'alam (proper noun by convention), mutawati (univocal equal application), and mushakkik (gradational application with priority).

The Meaning of 'Meaning' and Demonstratives
Yazdi explains the division of singular terms based on unity of meaning and specification by convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why demonstratives are not 'alam according to the author, and analyze the two interpretations of 'meaning' in the division.

Core Idea
The restriction 'by convention' excludes terms that are only specified in usage, like demonstratives.

The commentator raises a subtle point about what is meant by 'meaning' in the division of singular terms. This discussion helps clarify the technical conditions for a term to be considered a proper noun ('alam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'meaning'
The commentator proposes two possibilities: (1) the actual object of the convention (al-mawdu' lahu haqiqatan), or (2) what the word is used for (ma ustu'mila fihi) including metaphorical usage.
2
Understand the consequence of each
If meaning is the actual object, then literal and figurative uses are separate categories. If meaning is what the word is used for, then demonstratives would have multiple meanings (universal convention, particular usage).
3
Apply to demonstratives
According to the author, 'this' is conventionally established for the universal 'present near masculine noun', but in usage it refers to a specific person. Thus, its specification comes from usage, not convention.
4
See why 'by convention' excludes them
The matn's restriction 'by convention' (wad'an) ensures that only terms specified by the original convention are 'alam. Demonstratives are not 'alam because their specification is from usage.
5
Summarize the discussion
The commentator shows that the restriction 'by convention' is necessary to exclude demonstratives, regardless of which interpretation of 'meaning' is adopted.

His statement: 'if equal' — meaning that the truth of this universal meaning upon those individuals is equal. His statement: 'if they differ' — meaning that the truth of this concept upon some individuals is prior ... (then additional commentary from Taqrib) ... (0) His statement: 'by convention' — meaning that specification comes to the meaning from the side of convention, not from usage. For what is conventionally established with a general convention and a general object but is specified in usage, like demonstratives according to the author where he claims that the word 'this' for example is conventionally established for the universal of the present near masculine noun, even though in usage it is only said to a specific person, it is not called a proper noun. (Taqrib p. 22) (ه) His statement: 'or priority' — like the variations that exist among individuals of one reality in intensity and weakness and increase.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage explains the division of singular terms (al-mufrad) into three types: proper noun ('alam), univocal (mutawati), and gradational (mushakkik). The matn states: if the meaning of a singular term is one, and it is specified by convention (i.e., its meaning is a particular individual by original convention), it is a proper noun. If it is not specified, then it is either mutawati if all its individuals equally fall under it, or mushakkik if some are prior or stronger in applying the term. The commentator expands: 'its meaning is one' means the term has a single meaning. 'Specification' means particularity (individuality). 'By convention' indicates that specification comes from the original assignment, not from usage. For example, demonstratives like 'this' have a universal meaning in the original convention but are specified in usage; according to the author, they are not proper nouns. A discussion follows: what is meant by 'meaning'? It could be the actual object of the convention (what the word is literally assigned to) or what the word is used for (including metaphorical usage). If it is the former, then literal and metaphorical uses are not part of the division of multiple meaning; if the latter, demonstratives would be considered words with multiple meanings (since their intended reference is particular but their conventional meaning is universal) and thus would need to be excluded, but the matn's restriction 'by convention' already excludes them. The commentator then clarifies the conditions for mutawati and mushakkik, providing examples: existence is mushakkik because it applies to God primarily and to creatures secondarily; whiteness is mushakkik because pure white is more intense than mixed white. Mutawati terms apply equally, like 'human' to all humans.

Key Terms
علم
وضع
استعمال
اشارة

C) It has multiple meanings.
D) It is a gradational term.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'by convention' excludes terms whose specification comes from usage, such as demonstratives, from being considered proper nouns.

Classifying Words with Multiple Meanings
Author classifies ambiguous words and distinguishes mushtarak, manqul, haqiqa, and majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a word from an Islamic science text into mushtarak, manqul, haqiqa, or majaz based on its original wad' and usage patterns.

Core Idea
A word's meaning depends on its original placement and whether a new meaning has replaced or coexists with it.

This passage from a classical logic commentary provides a rigorous framework for understanding words with multiple meanings. The author expands on a concise statement to distinguish between words that have multiple original placements (mushtarak), words whose prominence has shifted to a new meaning (manqul), and words used both literally and figuratively (haqiqa and majaz). Mastering these categories is essential for precise interpretation of legal and theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and list its meanings
Start with a word that has multiple usages in Islamic texts, like 'الصلاة' (salat) which means supplication and ritual prayer. Note all meanings known.
2
Check if each meaning has a separate original wad'
If the word was initially placed for each meaning by a separate act of wad', then it is mushtarak. For example, 'العين' (ayn) was placed for the eye, gold, and the self independently.
Mushtarak words are rare but important in legal reasoning.
3
If not mushtarak, determine if one meaning has become predominant and the original abandoned
If the word is now primarily used in a second meaning such that the first is no longer used without a qarina, then it is manqul. The second meaning is specified by the nāqil (shar'i, 'urfi, or istilahi). For example, 'الصلاة' in the Sharia refers to the ritual prayer, not supplication.
If the original meaning is still used, it is not manqul.
4
If neither mushtarak nor manqul, distinguish haqiqa from majaz
If the word is used in its original meaning, it is haqiqa. If used in a secondary meaning with a qarina (contextual clue), it is majaz. For instance, the word 'أسد' (asad) is haqiqa for lion, and majaz for a brave person when accompanied by a clue.
Majaz requires a qarina to prevent confusion with haqiqa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if it (the word) has many (meanings)? If it is placed for each (meaning) initially, then it is mushtarak. And if not? Then if it becomes famous in the second (meaning), then it is manqul, attributed to the nāqil. Otherwise, it is either haqiqa or majaz. His saying: 'And if it has many': i.e., the single word, if its meaning that is used in it is many, then it is either that it is placed for each one of those meanings initially, by a separate wad', or it is not so. The first is called mushtarak, like 'ayn for the seeing eye, for gold, and for the essence. And as for the second, then inevitably the word is placed for one of those meanings, since the single word is a part of the placed word. Then if it is used in another meaning? If it becomes famous in this second meaning and its usage in the first meaning is abandoned, such that the second meaning comes to mind when it is uttered without a qarīna, then this is called manqul. And if it does not become famous in the second and is not abandoned in the first, but rather it is used sometimes in the first and sometimes in the second? Then if it is used in the first, i.e. the meaning for which it was placed, the word is called haqiqa. And if it is used in the second, which is not the meaning for which it was placed, it is called majaz. Then know that manqul necessarily has a nāqil from the first meaning (the manqul minhu) to the second meaning (the manqul ilayhi). This nāqil is either the people of the Sharia, or the people of common custom, or the people of specific custom and especially technical terminology, like the grammarians, for example. According to the first, it is called shar'i manqul; according to the second, 'urfi manqul; and according to the third, istilahi manqul. And to this he (the author) pointed with his saying: 'attributed to the nāqil.'
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of key terms related to words with multiple meanings, expanding on a concise matn statement. The author begins by stating a conditional classification: if a word has multiple meanings, then either it is placed for each meaning initially (mushtarak) or not. If not, then if it becomes famous in a second meaning to the point where the first meaning is abandoned and the second comes to mind without context, it is manqul (transferred). Otherwise, the word is either haqiqa (literal) or majaz (figurative). The commentary then unpacks 'wa in kathura', explaining that if a word's usage involves multiple meanings, it either has separate wad' for each (mushtarak, e.g., 'ayn) or only one original wad'. Then three categories are distinguished: (1) mushtarak: multiple original meanings; (2) manqul: transferred usage where the second meaning becomes predominant, often with the first abandoned; (3) haqiqa and majaz: when the word is used both in its original meaning (haqiqa) and in a secondary meaning (majaz) with a clue. Finally, the passage discusses the necessity of a nāqil (transporter) for manqul, categorizing it based on the source: Sharia, common custom, or specific convention (e.g., grammar). All examples are drawn from the Islamic intellectual tradition, such as 'ayn for the eye, gold, and essence.

Key Takeaway

Partial Contrast and the Relation of Negatives
Explains partial contrast and the relation of negatives in term pairings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify partial contrast between two terms and determine the relation between their contradictories (negatives).

Core Idea
Partial contrast is the middle relation between overlap and full exclusion, and it governs how negatives relate.

In Islamic logic, the relations between terms—such as general-special from one perspective and full contrast—determine how their negatives (contradictories) relate. This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya clarifies partial contrast and shows that the contradictories of a pair of terms can have a different relation than the originals. Understanding this is essential for valid reasoning about definitions and classes.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three term relations
Terms can be related by general-special from one perspective (partial overlap), full contrast (no overlap), or partial contrast (sometimes overlap, sometimes not). Review these from the five universals.
Partial contrast is the least strict; it covers cases that are not consistently overlapping or completely exclusive.
2
Define partial contrast from the passage
The author states: 'Partial contrast occurs within general-special from one perspective and also within full contrast.' This means partial contrast is a broader category that includes both types of relations between terms when they are not always coincident or always separate.
Example: 'animal' and 'white' have general-special from one perspective, which is a kind of partial contrast. 'human' and 'stone' have full contrast, but their contradictories have partial overlap, so the contradictories are in partial contrast.
3
Analyze the contradictories of terms with general-special from one perspective
Take terms like 'animal' and 'white' (general-special from one perspective). Their contradictories 'not-animal' and 'not-white' also have general-special from one perspective. However, for 'animal' and 'non-human' (general-special from one perspective), the contradictories 'not-animal' and 'human' have full contrast. So the relation of contradictories can be either partial contrast or full contrast.
The author emphasizes that the contradictories of the more general and more specific from one perspective have a partial contrast, not exclusively one relation.
4
Verify the claim with examples
Check the example: 'existent' and 'non-existent' have full contrast, and their contradictories also have full contrast. 'human' and 'stone' have full contrast, but their contradictories 'not-human' and 'not-stone' have general-special from one perspective, which is a form of partial contrast. Thus, partial contrast is the common result.
This shows that the relation between contradictories is not simply the opposite of the original relation; it depends on the specific terms.
5
Understand why the author postponed the discussion
The author notes two reasons: brevity by analogy, and the need to first understand the two specific individuals (instances) of partial contrast—general-special from one perspective and full contrast—before grasping the abstract concept.
This pedagogical move highlights the importance of concrete examples in learning logical relations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Like the two contrasting terms, and they are the other in the sentence. For neither applies to both simultaneously with the contradictory of the other; rather, each of the two contradictories applies together with the other actually, so each of the two contradictories applies without the other in the sentence, and that is the partial contrast. Then, this partial contrast may occur within full contrast, like 'existent' and 'non-existent', for between their two contradictories, which are 'not-existent' and 'not-non-existent', there is also full contrast. The second: that the conceptualization of partial contrast inasmuch as it is abstracted from its two individuals is dependent on the conceptualization of its two individuals, which are general-special from one perspective and full contrast. Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the relation of partial contrast (al-tabayun al-juz'i) and how it applies to the contradictories (naqidan) of terms that have a general-special from one perspective (al-'amm wa al-khass min wajh). The author begins by clarifying that when two terms are both true together, they have a relation of general-special from one perspective; if they never coincide, they have full contrast. Partial contrast is the relation where two terms are sometimes true together and sometimes not, and it occurs within both of these larger categories. The author then examines the contradictories of such terms. For example, 'animal' and 'white' have a general-special from one perspective (some animals are white, some are not). Their contradictories, 'not-animal' and 'not-white', also have this relation because something can be not-animal and not-white (e.g., a stone) or not-animal but white (e.g., snow) and so on. In contrast, the contradictories of 'animal' and 'non-human' ('not-animal' and 'human') have full contrast because nothing is both not-animal and human. The key point is that the relation between the contradictories of two terms that have general-special from one perspective is itself a partial contrast, not general-special or full contrast exclusively. The author then explains the commentator's phrase 'like the two contrasting terms' by comparing the contradictories of the more general and more specific from one perspective with those of two entirely contrasting terms. The logic is that neither contradictory applies to the other's actual instance, but each applies without the other in some cases. Next, the author shows that partial contrast can manifest either within full contrast (e.g., 'existent' and 'non-existent' have contradictories that directly oppose) or within general-special from one perspective (e.g., 'human' and 'stone' have contradictories that partially overlap). Finally, the author notes that the writer delayed discussion of the contradictories of contrasting terms for two reasons: first, to abbreviate by analogy; second, because understanding abstract partial contrast requires prior understanding of its two instances: general-special from one perspective and full contrast. All examples are drawn from the Islamic tradition, such as 'animal', 'human', 'white', and 'existent'.

The Differentia's Two Relations: Constitutive and Divisive
Commentator explains the two relationships of the differentia as constitutive and divisive, and that what constitutes the higher constitutes the lower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the constitutive and divisive functions of the differentia and apply the principle that what constitutes the higher constitutes the lower.

Core Idea
The differentia both constitutes the species and divides the genus—two sides of the same coin.

The differentia (fasl) is the essential attribute that distinguishes a species. But it plays two roles: it constitutes the essence of the species, and it divides the genus into two parts. This dual nature is key to understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic. Let's unpack it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations of the differentia
Look at the first sentence: the differentia relates to the species it distinguishes (constitutive) and to the genus it distinguishes from (divisive). Find these in the Arabic.
The passage uses 'مقوم' and 'مقسم'.
2
Understand 'constitutive' (muqawwim)
When the differentia is part of the species' essence, it is called constitutive. For example, 'rational' is part of 'human'. Verify this with the commentary's example.
It says 'جزء ذاتى' (intrinsic part).
3
Understand 'divisive' (muqassim)
When the differentia is related to the genus, it divides it. 'Rational' divides 'animal' into rational and non-rational. See the example in the margin.
The division is into two groups: with and without the differentia.
4
Apply the principle: what constitutes the higher constitutes the lower
The commentary explains that a differentia constituting a higher genus (e.g., 'growing' for growing body) also constitutes lower species (e.g., animal and human) because the higher is part of the lower. Trace this using the example.
Higher and lower are relative; intermediate genera are both higher and lower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اذا نسب (ه) الى ما يميزه فيقوم و الى ما يميزه عنه والمقوم للعالى () مقوم السافل. الحاشية: فمقسم. في جنسه البعيد وهو الجسم النامي. الى قوله واذ انسب»: الفصل له نسبة الى الماهية التي هو فصل مميز لها ونسبة الجنس الذي يميز الماهية عنه من بين افراده فهو بالاعتبار الاول يسمى مقوماً (۱) (۱۱۱) لأنه جزء للماهية و محصل لها و بالاعتبار الثاني يسمى مقسماً لانه بانضمامه الى هذا الجنس وجوداً يحصل قسماً وعدماً يحصل قسما آخر كما ترى في تقسيم الحيوان الى الحيوان الناطق و الحيوان الغير الناطق. قوله « والمقوم للعالى» اللام للاستغراق (۱۱۲) ای کل فصل مقوم للعالى (۱۱۳) فهو فصل مقوم للسافل لان مقوم العالى جزء العالى والعالى جزء للسافل وجزء الجزء جزء (۱۱۴) فتقوم العالى جزء للسافل . ثم انه يميز السافل عن كل ما يميز العالى عنه فيكون جزء مميزاً له و هو معنى المقوم. وليعلم ان المراد بالعالى هيهنا (۱۱۵) كل جنس او نوع يكون فوق آخر سواء كان فوقه آخر او لم يكن وكذا المراد بالسافل كل جنس او نوع يكون تحت آخر سواء كان تحته آخر او لم يكن حتى ان الجنس المتوسط عال بالنسبة الى ما تحته وسافل بالنسبة الى ما فوقه. (ه) قوله واذا انسب»: اى الفصل الى النوع الذي يميزه عن الانواع المشتركة معه فى جنس، فالفصل من هذه الناحية يقال له: «مقوم» لأنه جزء ذاتى للنوع و الجزء من مقومات ماتركب منه . و اذا نسب الى الجنس الذي ميز عن سائر انواعه نوعاً بخصوصه فالفصل بهذا الاعتبار يقال له: «مقسم» لانه قسم الجنس الى قسم فيه هذا الفصل والفارق و قسم ليس فيه هذا الفصل والفارق، مثلاً الناطق اذا نسب الى الانسان فهو مقوم له ، لانه ذاتى من ذاتياته واذا نسب الى الحيوان الذي ميز عن سائر انواعه هذا النوع المخصوص وهو الانسان فهو مقسم له الى حيوان ناطق و هو الانسان وحيوان غير ناطق وهو غير الانسان (التقريب ص ۳۵). (و) قال والمقوم للعالى»: كالنامي المقوم للجسم النامي الذي هو الشجر وغيره من كل جسم له نمو، مقوم للسافل الذى هو الحيوان و الانسان ايضاً، فان الحيوان والانسان مما يشتركان في الجسم مع غيرهما من الاجسام فاذا تميز الجسم النامي عن غيره من انواع الاجسام بالنامي فقد تميز الحيوان والانسان ايضاً عن سائر انواع الاجسام بانهما ناميان لان الجسم النامي جزء من الحيوان و من الانسان، فالميزة التي تكون نصيبه نصيب ما تركب منه ايضاً و من جملة ما تركب منه الحيوان والانسان (التقريب ص ۳۵)
And when it (the differentia) is related to what it distinguishes (the species), it is constitutive (muqawwim), and when related to what it distinguishes it from (the genus), it is divisive (muqassim). And what constitutes the higher constitutes the lower. The marginal commentary: So it is divisive. In its remote genus, which is the growing body (jism namī). Up to his saying 'and when it is related': The differentia has a relation to the essence (māhiyya) of which it is the distinguishing differentia, and a relation to the genus which distinguishes the essence from among its individuals. So according to the first consideration it is called constitutive (muqawwim) because it is a part of the essence and its actualizer, and according to the second consideration it is called divisive (muqassim) because by its conjunction with this genus, existence yields a division and non-existence yields another division, as you see in the division of 'animal' into 'rational animal' and 'irrational animal'. His saying 'and what constitutes the higher': the lām is for generality, meaning every differentia that constitutes the higher is a differentia that constitutes the lower, because the constitutive of the higher is a part of the higher, and the higher is a part of the lower, and a part of a part is a part, so the constitutive of the higher is a part of the lower. Then it distinguishes the lower from everything from which the higher distinguishes it, so it becomes a distinguishing part for it, and that is the meaning of constitutive. And let it be known that what is meant by 'higher' here is every genus or species that is above another, whether there is another above it or not, and similarly what is meant by 'lower' is every genus or species that is under another, whether there is another under it or not, so that the intermediate genus is higher relative to what is under it and lower relative to what is above it. (H) His saying 'and when it is related': meaning the differentia to the species that it distinguishes from the species sharing its genus. So the differentia from this aspect is called 'constitutive' because it is an intrinsic part of the species, and the part is among the constituents of that which is composed of it. And when it is related to the genus from which it distinguishes a particular species, the differentia in this respect is called 'divisive' because it divides the genus into a division containing this differentia and its differential, and a division not containing this differentia and its differential. For example, 'rational' when related to 'human' is constitutive for it, because it is intrinsic among its intrinsics, and when related to 'animal' from which it distinguishes this particular species, namely 'human', it is divisive for it into rational animal (which is human) and non-rational animal (which is non-human). (W) He said 'and what constitutes the higher': such as 'growing' which constitutes the growing body, which is tree and other every body having growth, is also constitutive of the lower which is animal and human. For animal and human share in body with other bodies. So when the growing body is distinguished from other types of bodies by 'growing', animal and human are also distinguished from all other types of bodies by being growing, because the growing body is a part of animal and human. So the distinguishing trait that is its portion is also the portion of what is composed of it, and among what animal and human are composed of.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on a foundational matn in logic, explaining the dual role of the differentia (fasl). The differentia has two relations: to the species it constitutes, and to the genus it divides. When considered in relation to the species, it is called muqawwim (constitutive) because it is an essential part that actualizes the essence. When considered in relation to the genus, it is called muqassim (divisive) because it splits the genus into two divisions: those that have the differentia and those that do not. The commentator then explains the principle that what constitutes the higher (like the differentia of a superior genus) also constitutes the lower (the species under it). This is because the higher is a part of the lower, and the part of a part is a part. For example, 'growing' (nāmi) constitutes the growing body (jism nāmi) and also constitutes animal and human, since the growing body is part of animal. The passage also clarifies the definitions of 'higher' and 'lower' in this context: any genus or species above another is higher, and any below is lower, including intermediate genera. The commentator uses the example of 'rational' (nāṭiq) to illustrate: relative to human, it is constitutive; relative to animal, it is divisive. The core takeaway is that the differentia's function depends on its relational context, and the constitutive property of higher genera necessarily carries down to lower species.

Key Takeaway
The differentia has two functions: constitutive (relative to the species) and divisive (relative to the genus), and what constitutes a higher genus also constitutes all lower species under it.

The Existence of the Natural Universal
Debate on the external existence of the natural universal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of universals (logical, mental, natural) and evaluate the argument against the external existence of the natural universal.

Core Idea
The natural universal does not exist externally; only its individuals exist.

In Islamic logic and metaphysics, the status of universals is a foundational question. Are they real entities in the external world, or merely mental constructs? This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya refines the debate by distinguishing three types of universals and presenting a decisive argument that the natural universal (like 'humanity') has no existence outside the mind – only its individual instances (Zayd, 'Amr, etc.) exist. Understanding this distinction is crucial for theology (e.g., divine attributes) and jurisprudence (e.g., universal rulings).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universals
The passage mentions three universals: the logical universal (al-kulli al-mantiqi), the mental universal (al-kulli al-'aqli), and the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i). Note how each is defined: the logical universal is universality itself, a secondary intelligible; the mental universal is the concept formed in the mind; the natural universal is the common nature considered in itself.
This tripartite distinction is standard in post-Avicennan logic.
2
Understand why logical and mental universals are not external
The author quickly dismisses their external existence: the logical universal is an accident of concepts in the mind (a secondary intelligible), so it cannot be external. The mental universal, being a whole consisting of parts, cannot exist externally because if its parts are mental, the whole is mental too.
The principle used: negation of a part entails negation of the whole.
3
Analyze the argument against the natural universal existing externally
The main argument: if the natural universal (e.g., 'human') existed externally in individuals, then one and the same nature would be described with opposite attributes (e.g., tall and short) and would exist in multiple places simultaneously – both impossible. Therefore, the universal cannot exist externally.
This is a valid conditional argument (modus tollens).
4
Conclude the meaning of 'existence of the natural universal'
The author concludes that 'the existence of the natural universal' means nothing more than the existence of its individuals. There is no extra entity in the external world beyond the concrete particulars.
This position aligns with nominalism in the Islamic context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والحق ان وجود الطبيعى بمعنى وجود اشخاصه. لا ينبغي ان يشك فى ان الكلى المنطقى غير موجود فى الخارج فان الكلية أنها تعرض للمفهومات في العقل و لذا كانت من المعقولات الثانية و كذا فى ان الكلى العقلى غير موجود فيه فان انتفاء الجزء يستلزم انتفاء الكل و انما النزاع في ان الطبيعي من حيث هو انسان الذى يعرضه الكلية فى العقل هل هو موجود في الخارج بوجود افراده ام لا بل ليس الموجود فيه الا الافراد؟ والاول مذهب الحكماء والثاني مذهب بعض المتأخرين و منهم المصنف و لذا قال: الحق هو الثانى و ذلك لانه لو وجد الكلى فى الخارج فى ضمن الافراد لزم اتصاف الشيء الواحد بالصفات المتضادة ووجود الشيء الواحد فى الامكنة المتعددة وحينئذ فمعنى وجود الطبيعي هو ان افراده موجودة.
He said: 'And the truth is that the existence of the natural (universal) is in the sense of the existence of its individuals.' It should not be doubted that the logical universal does not exist in the external world, because universality attaches to concepts in the mind, and thus it is among the secondary intelligibles. Likewise, the mental universal does not exist externally, for the negation of the part entails the negation of the whole. The only dispute is concerning the natural (universal) insofar as it is human, which is subject to universality in the mind: does it exist in the external world through the existence of its individuals, or not, rather nothing exists externally except the individuals? The first is the view of the philosophers (al-hukama'), and the second is the view of some later scholars, among them the author (of the matn), and therefore he said: 'The truth is the second.' This is because if the universal existed in the external world within the individuals, it would necessitate that one thing be described with opposite attributes and that one thing exist in multiple places. On this basis, the meaning of the existence of the natural (universal) is that its individuals exist.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage addresses a key metaphysical debate: does the natural universal (al-kulli al-tabi'i) have any existence outside the mind? The author first dismisses the external existence of the logical and mental universals: the logical universal is a concept attached in the mind, so it is a secondary intelligible; the mental universal is a composite whose parts are mental, so it cannot be external. The real debate is about the natural universal, i.e., the common nature (like 'humanity') as it exists in individuals. The philosophers (al-hukama') hold that the natural universal exists externally through the existence of its individuals, meaning that the common nature is somehow present in each individual. The later scholars (including the author of the text being commented on) hold that nothing exists externally except the individuals themselves; the common nature is only a mental consideration. The author of this Hashiya (Yazdi) sides with the later scholars, arguing that if the universal existed externally in individuals, then the same thing (the universal nature) would be described with opposite attributes (e.g., Zayd is tall, 'Amr is short) and would exist in multiple places, which is impossible. Therefore, 'the existence of the natural universal' means merely that its individuals exist.

معقولات ثانية

Key Takeaway
The natural universal has no external existence; only its individuals exist, and the term 'existence of the natural universal' is a shorthand for the existence of its instances.
