Conditions of a Valid Definition
Explanation of conditions for a valid definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a proposed definition satisfies the conditions of coextensiveness and greater clarity.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be coextensive with the definiendum and clearer than it.

Before we examine the types of definition, we must first understand the foundational conditions that every definition must meet. This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya explains why a definition cannot be broader, narrower, or equally vague as the term being defined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definer
Locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the term or phrase used to define it (definer). For example, when defining 'man', the definer might be 'animal' or 'rational animal'.
The passage uses the example of 'animal' defining 'man'.
2
Check for coextensiveness
Determine if the definer applies to exactly the same set of things as the definiendum. The passage shows that 'animal' is broader (includes horses), so it fails. The definer must be equal in extension (musawi).
Coextensiveness is the first condition.
3
Check for greater clarity
The definer must be more known (ajla) to the intellect than the definiendum. A narrower term like 'rational animal' might be less known, so it also fails. The definer should lead from known to unknown.
Clarity is the second condition.
4
Evaluate the definition
Based on the checks, conclude whether the definition is valid. If the definer is coextensive and clearer, it is valid. Otherwise, identify which condition it violates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: معرف الشيء ما يقال عليه لافادة .تصوره. ويشترط ان يكون مساوياً و اجلي فلا يصح بالاعم والاخص و المساوى معرفة و الاخفى والتعريف بالفصل القريب حد و بالخاصة فان كان مع رسم المعرف الجنس القريب فتام عن جميع قوله «معرف الشيء»: بعد الفراغ عن بيان ما يتركب منه المعرف (1) شرع فى البحث عنه وقد علمت ان المقصود بالذات في هذا الفن هو البحث عنه وعن الحجة وعرفه بانه ما يحمل على الشيء اى المُعرَّف ليفيد تصور هذا الشيء (٢) اما : بكنهه (٣) او بوجه يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه (٤) و لهذا لم يجز ان يكون اعم لان الاعم لا يفيد شيئاً منهما كالحيوان فى تعريف الانسان فان الحيوان ليس كنه الانسان لان الانسان هو الحيوان الناطق و ايضاً لا يميز الانسان ما عداه لان بعض الحيوان هو الفرس وكذا الحال فى الاعم من وجه واما الاخص اعنى مطلقاً (٥) فهو وان جازان يفيد تصوره تصور الاعم بالكنه (٦) او بوجه يمتاز عما عداه كما اذا تصورت الانسان بانه حیوان ناطق فقد تصورت فى ضمنه الحيوان باحد الوجهين (٧) لكن لما كان الاخص اقل وجوداً في العقل (٨) و اخفى فى نظره و شأن المعرف ان يكون اعرف من المعرف لم يجز ان يكون اخص ايضاً وقد علم من تعريف المعرف بما يحمل على الشيء (٩) انه لا يجوز ان يكون المعرف مبايناً للمعرف فتعين ان يكون مساو ياله في الصدق. ثم ينبغى ان يكون المعرّف اعرف من المعرّف (١٠) فى نظر العقل لانه معلوم موصل إلى تصور مجهول هو المعرّف لا اخفى منه ولا مساوياً له في الخفاء والظهور (١١) قوله بالفصل القريب حد التعريف لابد ان يشتمل على امر يخص المعرف ويساويه بناء على ما سبق من اشتراط المساواة (١٢) فهذا الامر ان كان ذاتياً كان فصلاً قريباً (١٣) و ان كان عرضياً كان خاصة لا محالة (١٤) فعلى الاول المعرف حداً (١٥) وعلى الثانى يسمى رسماً ثم كل منهما ان اشتمل على الجنس القريب يسمى حداً تاماً و رسماً تاماً (١٦) و ان لم يشتمل على الجنس القريب سواء اشتمل على الجنس البعيد او كان هناك فصل قريب وحده او خاصة وحدها (١٧) يسمى ناقصاً ورسماً ناقصاً (١٨) هذا محصل كلامهم (١٩) وفيه ابحاث لا يسعها المقام.
Chapter: The definer of a thing is what is said about it to benefit the conception of it. It is required to be coextensive and more evident, so it is not correct with the more general, the more specific, and the coextensive unknown. Definition by the proximate differentia is called a definition (hadd), by the property a description (rasm); if it is with the proximate genus, it is complete (tamm). His statement: 'the definer of a thing': after finishing the explanation of what the definer is composed of, he started the investigation of it. You have known that the primary object in this art is the investigation of it and of the argument. He defined it as what is predicated of the thing—i.e., the definiendum—to benefit of the conception of this thing, either by its essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from all others. Therefore, it is not permissible for it to be more general, because the more general does not benefit either of the two, like 'animal' in defining 'man'; for 'animal' is not the essence of 'man', because 'man' is 'rational animal', and also does not distinguish 'man' from others because some animals are horses. Similarly is the case with the more general in some respects. As for the more specific, i.e., absolutely, even if it is possible that its conception gives conception of the more general by essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from others, as when you conceive 'man' as 'rational animal', you have conceived 'animal' in its wake by one of the two ways, yet because the more specific is less existent in the mind and more hidden in its consideration, and the definer should be more known than the definiendum, it is not permissible for it to be more specific either. And it is known from the definition of the definer as what is predicated of the thing that the definer must not be disparate from the definiendum, so it is determined that it must be coextensive in truth. Then the definer should be more known than the definiendum in the view of the intellect, because it is something known leading to the conception of something unknown which is the definiendum, neither more hidden than it nor coextensive with it in hiddenness and clarity. His statement: 'by the proximate differentia, a definition (hadd)'. The definition must include something that specifies the definiendum and equals it, based on the previous condition of coextensiveness. So this thing, if it is essential, it is a proximate differentia, and if it is accidental, it is necessarily a property. So on the first, the definer is called a definition (hadd), and on the second it is called a description (rasm). Then each of them, if it includes the proximate genus, it is called a complete definition (hadd tamm) and a complete description (rasm tamm); and if it does not include the proximate genus—whether it includes the remote genus or there is a proximate differentia alone or a property alone—it is called an incomplete definition (hadd naqis) and an incomplete description (rasm naqis). This is the gist of their statements. There are investigations in it that cannot be accommodated here.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the topic of definition (ta'rif). It begins by stating the definition of 'definer' (mu'arraf) as a term predicated of the definiendum to produce conception, either of its essence or of a distinguishing mark. The text then outlines the necessary conditions: the definer must be coextensive (musawi) with the definiendum (not broader or narrower) and more evident (ajla) than it. It explains why broader terms fail (e.g., 'animal' for 'man' does not give essence nor distinguish), and why narrower terms also fail (though they could in principle give conception of the broader, they are less familiar and should not be used because the definer must be better known). It also excludes terms that are completely disparate. After these conditions, the text moves to types: if the definer uses a proximate essential differentia (fasad qarib), it is a 'hadd' (definition); if it uses an accidental property (kassa), it is a 'rasm' (description). Furthermore, each may be 'tamm' (complete) if it includes the proximate genus alongside the differentia/property, or 'naqis' (incomplete) if it omits the proximate genus, using only the differentia/property alone or with a remote genus. The passage concludes that this is the gist of the logicians' position, noting that more detailed investigations remain.

معرف
مساوي
اجلى

A) Because 'animal' is coextensive with 'man'
B) Because 'animal' is broader than 'man'

D) Because 'animal' is equally evident as 'man'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be exactly coextensive with the definiendum and more evident to the intellect.

Types of Definition: Hadd, Rasm, Tamm, and Naqis
Classification of definitions into hadd/rasm and tamm/naqis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given definition as hadd or rasm, and as tamm or naqis.

Core Idea
Definitions using essential differentia are called hadd; those using accidental property are rasm; including proximate genus makes them tamm.

After establishing the conditions for a valid definition, the text now classifies definitions based on the kind of differentia used and whether the proximate genus is included. This classification is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differentia
Look for the characteristic that specifies the definiendum. In the definition 'rational animal', the differentia is 'rational'. Determine whether it is essential (fasl qarib) or accidental (kassa).
Essential differentia is part of the essence; accidental property is not.
2
Classify as hadd or rasm
If the differentia is essential, the definition is a hadd (definition proper). If it is accidental, it is a rasm (description).
The passage states this clearly.
3
Check for the proximate genus
Does the definition include the proximate genus (jins qarib)? For example, 'animal' is the proximate genus for 'man'. If included, the definition is tamm (complete). If not (only differentia or property, or a remote genus), it is naqis (incomplete).
The passage provides four categories: hadd tamm, hadd naqis, rasm tamm, rasm naqis.
4
Apply to examples
Classify 'rational animal' for man: differentia = rational (essential), includes proximate genus = animal → hadd tamm. Classify 'laughing animal': differentia = laughing (accidental property), includes genus → rasm tamm. Classify 'rational' alone: differentia essential but no genus → hadd naqis.

فصل: معرف الشيء ما يقال عليه لافادة .تصوره. ويشترط ان يكون مساوياً و اجلي فلا يصح بالاعم والاخص و المساوى معرفة و الاخفى والتعريف بالفصل القريب حد و بالخاصة فان كان مع رسم المعرف الجنس القريب فتام عن جميع قوله «معرف الشيء»: بعد الفراغ عن بيان ما يتركب منه المعرف (1) شرع فى البحث عنه وقد علمت ان المقصود بالذات في هذا الفن هو البحث عنه وعن الحجة وعرفه بانه ما يحمل على الشيء اى المُعرَّف ليفيد تصور هذا الشيء (٢) اما : بكنهه (٣) او بوجه يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه (٤) و لهذا لم يجز ان يكون اعم لان الاعم لا يفيد شيئاً منهما كالحيوان فى تعريف الانسان فان الحيوان ليس كنه الانسان لان الانسان هو الحيوان الناطق و ايضاً لا يميز الانسان ما عداه لان بعض الحيوان هو الفرس وكذا الحال فى الاعم من وجه واما الاخص اعنى مطلقاً (٥) فهو وان جازان يفيد تصوره تصور الاعم بالكنه (٦) او بوجه يمتاز عما عداه كما اذا تصورت الانسان بانه حیوان ناطق فقد تصورت فى ضمنه الحيوان باحد الوجهين (٧) لكن لما كان الاخص اقل وجوداً في العقل (٨) و اخفى فى نظره و شأن المعرف ان يكون اعرف من المعرف لم يجز ان يكون اخص ايضاً وقد علم من تعريف المعرف بما يحمل على الشيء (٩) انه لا يجوز ان يكون المعرف مبايناً للمعرف فتعين ان يكون مساو ياله في الصدق. ثم ينبغى ان يكون المعرّف اعرف من المعرّف (١٠) فى نظر العقل لانه معلوم موصل إلى تصور مجهول هو المعرّف لا اخفى منه ولا مساوياً له في الخفاء والظهور (١١) قوله بالفصل القريب حد التعريف لابد ان يشتمل على امر يخص المعرف ويساويه بناء على ما سبق من اشتراط المساواة (١٢) فهذا الامر ان كان ذاتياً كان فصلاً قريباً (١٣) و ان كان عرضياً كان خاصة لا محالة (١٤) فعلى الاول المعرف حداً (١٥) وعلى الثانى يسمى رسماً ثم كل منهما ان اشتمل على الجنس القريب يسمى حداً تاماً و رسماً تاماً (١٦) و ان لم يشتمل على الجنس القريب سواء اشتمل على الجنس البعيد او كان هناك فصل قريب وحده او خاصة وحدها (١٧) يسمى ناقصاً ورسماً ناقصاً (١٨) هذا محصل كلامهم (١٩) وفيه ابحاث لا يسعها المقام.
Chapter: The definer of a thing is what is said about it to benefit the conception of it. It is required to be coextensive and more evident, so it is not correct with the more general, the more specific, and the coextensive unknown. Definition by the proximate differentia is called a definition (hadd), by the property a description (rasm); if it is with the proximate genus, it is complete (tamm). His statement: 'the definer of a thing': after finishing the explanation of what the definer is composed of, he started the investigation of it. You have known that the primary object in this art is the investigation of it and of the argument. He defined it as what is predicated of the thing—i.e., the definiendum—to benefit of the conception of this thing, either by its essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from all others. Therefore, it is not permissible for it to be more general, because the more general does not benefit either of the two, like 'animal' in defining 'man'; for 'animal' is not the essence of 'man', because 'man' is 'rational animal', and also does not distinguish 'man' from others because some animals are horses. Similarly is the case with the more general in some respects. As for the more specific, i.e., absolutely, even if it is possible that its conception gives conception of the more general by essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from others, as when you conceive 'man' as 'rational animal', you have conceived 'animal' in its wake by one of the two ways, yet because the more specific is less existent in the mind and more hidden in its consideration, and the definer should be more known than the definiendum, it is not permissible for it to be more specific either. And it is known from the definition of the definer as what is predicated of the thing that the definer must not be disparate from the definiendum, so it is determined that it must be coextensive in truth. Then the definer should be more known than the definiendum in the view of the intellect, because it is something known leading to the conception of something unknown which is the definiendum, neither more hidden than it nor coextensive with it in hiddenness and clarity. His statement: 'by the proximate differentia, a definition (hadd)'. The definition must include something that specifies the definiendum and equals it, based on the previous condition of coextensiveness. So this thing, if it is essential, it is a proximate differentia, and if it is accidental, it is necessarily a property. So on the first, the definer is called a definition (hadd), and on the second it is called a description (rasm). Then each of them, if it includes the proximate genus, it is called a complete definition (hadd tamm) and a complete description (rasm tamm); and if it does not include the proximate genus—whether it includes the remote genus or there is a proximate differentia alone or a property alone—it is called an incomplete definition (hadd naqis) and an incomplete description (rasm naqis). This is the gist of their statements. There are investigations in it that cannot be accommodated here.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the topic of definition (ta'rif). It begins by stating the definition of 'definer' (mu'arraf) as a term predicated of the definiendum to produce conception, either of its essence or of a distinguishing mark. The text then outlines the necessary conditions: the definer must be coextensive (musawi) with the definiendum (not broader or narrower) and more evident (ajla) than it. It explains why broader terms fail (e.g., 'animal' for 'man' does not give essence nor distinguish), and why narrower terms also fail (though they could in principle give conception of the broader, they are less familiar and should not be used because the definer must be better known). It also excludes terms that are completely disparate. After these conditions, the text moves to types: if the definer uses a proximate essential differentia (fasad qarib), it is a 'hadd' (definition); if it uses an accidental property (kassa), it is a 'rasm' (description). Furthermore, each may be 'tamm' (complete) if it includes the proximate genus alongside the differentia/property, or 'naqis' (incomplete) if it omits the proximate genus, using only the differentia/property alone or with a remote genus. The passage concludes that this is the gist of the logicians' position, noting that more detailed investigations remain.

Hadd and Rasm

The Two Conditions of a Valid Definition
Author defines ma'rif and states its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given definition satisfies the conditions of equality in scope and superior clarity.

Core Idea
A definition must be equal in scope and more evident than the thing defined.

The passage lays down two crucial conditions for any valid definition: it must be exactly coextensive with the definiendum and it must be better known. If a definiens is broader or narrower, it fails to convey the essence or to distinguish the thing. Similarly, if it is less known, it cannot lead from known to unknown. This lesson unpacks these conditions and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Take a proposed definition and isolate the term being defined (definiendum) and the defining expression (definiens). For example, defining 'human' (insān) as 'rational animal'.
2
Check the scope: is the definiens equal in truth-value?
Test whether the definiens applies to exactly the same things as the definiendum. If it applies to more (a'amm) or fewer (akhass), it fails. 'Animal' is broader than 'human' because it includes horses; 'rational' is narrower because not all animals are rational.
3
Check the clarity: is the definiens more evident?
The definiens must be better known in the mind than the definiendum. If it is more hidden (akhfā), it cannot illuminate the unknown. For instance, defining 'animal' by 'risible' is invalid because 'risible' is less familiar.
The condition of being more known is relative to the listener's conceptual framework.
4
Apply both conditions to an Islamic definition
Consider defining 'ṣalāh' as 'statements and actions beginning with takbīr and ending with taslīm'. This is equal in scope (covers all valid prayers) and more evident than an abstract definition? Evaluate whether it satisfies both conditions.

Chapter: The definition of a thing is what is said about it to give knowledge of its conceptualization. It is required that it be equal [in scope] and more evident, so it is not valid with a more general or more specific [term], and the equal is a definition, and the more hidden [is not]. And definition by the proximate differentia is a 'hadd', and by the special property [is a 'rasm']; if it is with the proximate genus, it is complete, otherwise incomplete. And they did not consider the common accident. And it has been permitted in the incomplete [definition] that it be more general, like the linguistic, which is intended to interpret the meaning of the word. 
His statement 'the definition of a thing': After finishing the exposition of what the definiens is composed of, he began to research it. And you have known that the essential purpose in this art is the research into it and into the proof. And he defined it as 'what is predicated of the thing, i.e., the definiendum, to give knowledge of the conceptualization of this thing, either by its essence or by a characteristic that distinguishes it from everything else. For this reason, it is not permissible that it be more general, because the more general does not provide either of these, like 'animal' in the definition of 'human'; for 'animal' is not the essence of 'human', because 'human' is 'rational animal', and also it does not distinguish 'human' from other things, because some 'animal' is 'horse'. And similarly for what is more general in some respect. As for the more specific—I mean absolutely—even though it may provide the conceptualization of the more general by its essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from others, as when you conceptualize 'human' as 'rational animal'—you have thereby conceptualized 'animal' in one of the two ways—nevertheless, because the more specific has less existence in the mind and is more hidden in its view, and the function of the definiens is to be more known than the definiendum, it is not permissible that it be more specific either. And it has been known from the definition of the definiens as 'what is predicated of the thing' that it is not permissible for the definiens to be opposite to the definiendum; so it is determined that it be equal in truth-value. Then it is necessary that the definiens be more known than the definiendum in the view of the mind, because it is a known thing leading to the conceptualization of an unknown thing which is the definiendum, not more hidden than it nor equal to it in hiddenness and manifestness. 
His statement 'by the proximate differentia is a hadd': The definition must include something that is specific to the definiendum and equal to it, based on what preceded of the condition of equality. So that thing, if it is essential, it is a proximate differentia; and if it is accidental, it is necessarily a special property. In the first case, the definiendum is called a 'hadd', and in the second it is called a 'rasm'. Then each of them, if it includes the proximate genus, is called a complete hadd and complete rasm; and if it does not include the proximate genus—whether it includes the remote genus, or there is a proximate differentia alone or a special property alone—it is called an incomplete hadd and incomplete rasm. This is the gist of their statement, and there are discussions that this place cannot accommodate. 
'In the definition' [heading] 51: 'and the incomplete, and they did not consider the common accident. And it has been permitted in the incomplete that it be more general, like the linguistic, which is intended to interpret the meaning of the word.' 
His statement 'and they did not consider the common accident': They said: the purpose of definition is either to attain knowledge of the essence of the definiendum or its distinction from everything else, and the common accident does not provide either of them; therefore they did not consider it in the context of definition. And the apparent meaning is that their purpose is that it is not considered in the context of definition individually. But definition by a set of things each of which is a common accident to the definiendum but the set is specific to it—like the definition of 'human' as 'walker with upright stature' and the definition of 'bat' as 'flying mammal'—that is a definition by a composite special property that is considered valid by them, as stated by some later scholars. 
His statement 'and it has been permitted in the incomplete': This is an indication of what the early scholars permitted, as they verified that it is permissible to define by the more general essential, like defining 'human' by 'animal'—thus it is an incomplete hadd—or by the more general accidental, like defining it by 'walker'—thus it is an incomplete rasm. Rather, they even permitted definition by the more specific accidental, like defining 'animal' by 'risible'. But the author (al-musannif) did not accept it, due to his view that it is a definition by what is more hidden, which is absolutely invalid. 
His statement 'like the linguistic': i.e., as it was permitted in linguistic definition that it be more general, like their saying: 'Sa'danah is a plant.' 
His statement 'interpretation of the meaning of the word': i.e., determining the referent in the mind; so there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known, as in the real definition—so understand.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the definition of definition (ma'rif) itself. It begins by stating that a definition is what is said about a thing to give conceptualization (tasawwur) of it. The author then lays down two conditions: the definition must be equal in scope (musāwin) and more evident (ajlā) than the definiendum. If a definition is broader (a'amm) or narrower (akhass), it fails to convey the essence or distinguish the thing from others. For example, defining 'human' as 'animal' is too broad because 'animal' does not capture the essence of rationality nor distinguish humans from horses. Similarly, defining by a narrower term is problematic because the narrower term is less familiar in the mind and is supposed to be more known than the definiendum, not less. The definition must also be more known than the definiendum; otherwise it cannot lead from the known to the unknown. Then the author distinguishes between two types of definition depending on what specific characteristic (khāṣṣ) is used: if it is an essential differentia (fasl qarīb), it is a hadd (logical definition); if it is an accidental property (khāṣṣa), it is a rasm (description). Each can be complete (tāmm) if it includes the proximate genus (jins qarīb), or incomplete (nāqiṣ) if it lacks the genus or uses a remote genus. Next, the commentator explains why the common accident (al-'araḍ al-'āmm) is not considered in definition—it does not reveal the essence nor uniquely distinguish the definiendum. However, a combination of common accidents that together are specific (like defining humans as 'bipedal walker with upright stature') is acceptable as a composite special property. Finally, the passage notes that early scholars allowed definitions by broader essential or accidental terms in incomplete definitions (e.g., defining human by 'animal' gives an incomplete hadd). They also allowed definition by a narrower accidental term (e.g., defining animal by 'risible'), but the author (likely al-Muṣannif) rejects this because such a term is more hidden than the definiendum, violating the condition of being more evident. The commentary concludes with a note on linguistic definition (tafsīr lafẓī), which can be broader because it only specifies the referent of a word, not providing new knowledge from the unknown.

مساو
أجلى

Why is defining 'human' as 'animal' not a valid definition according to this passage?

C) Because 'human' is more general than 'animal'.
D) Because 'animal' is a species of 'human'.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be exactly coextensive with its definiendum and better known than it; broader or narrower or less known terms fail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Test the following definition of 'zakāt': 'obligatory charity on specific wealth (like gold, silver, livestock) that has reached a minimum threshold (niṣāb) and passed one lunar year.' Does it satisfy the conditions of equality and being more evident? Why or why not?

Identify a definition from a fiqh text (e.g., of 'ṣawm' in fasting) and check whether the definiens is equal in scope to the definiendum and more evident.

Hadd and Rasm

Hadd and Rasm: Complete and Incomplete Definitions
Author distinguishes hadd and rasm with completeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition as hadd or rasm and determine whether it is complete or incomplete.

Core Idea
Definitions are either by essential differentia (hadd) or accidental property (rasm), and each may be complete or incomplete.

After establishing the general conditions of definition, the passage defines two types: hadd (logical definition) using the proximate differentia, and rasm (description) using a special accidental property. Each type can be complete if it includes the proximate genus, or incomplete if it lacks it. This distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences, where a complete hadd gives the essence, while a rasm only distinguishes.

Guided Path
1
Determine the type of defining property
Look at the specific element that distinguishes the definiendum: if it is an essential attribute (dhātī), it is a differentia (fasl), and the definition is a hadd. If it is an accidental attribute (araḍī), it is a special property (khāṣṣa), and the definition is a rasm.
Essential attributes are those that constitute the essence, like 'rational' for human; accidental attributes are contingent, like 'risible'.
2
Check for the presence of the proximate genus
Proximate genus (jins qarīb) is the immediate genus that together with the differentia forms the species. If the definition includes both proximate genus and differentia/property, it is complete (tāmm). If it omits the genus or uses a remote genus, it is incomplete (nāqiṣ).
3
Classify examples as complete/incomplete hadd and rasm
Example: 'Human is rational animal' is a complete hadd (proximate genus animal + differentia rational). 'Human is a risible animal' is a complete rasm (special property risible + genus). 'Human is risible' alone is an incomplete rasm (no genus).
The passage notes that early scholars allowed defining by a broader essential term alone (e.g., 'human is animal') as an incomplete hadd.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal definition
Consider the definition of 'ṣiyām' (fasting) as 'abstention from food, drink, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with the intention of worship'. The genus could be 'abstention' (common accident), and the differentia/special properties are 'from specified things, at specified times, with intention'. Is it a hadd or rasm? Complete or incomplete? Discuss.
This exercise requires you to decide whether the defining properties are essential or accidental to the concept of fasting.

فصل: معرف الشيء ما يقال عليه لافادة تصوره. ويشترط ان يكون مساوياً و اجلي فلا يصح بالاعم والاخص و المساوى معرفة و الاخفى والتعريف بالفصل القريب حد و بالخاصة فان كان مع الجنس القريب فتام و الافناقص و لم يعتبروا بالعرض العام. وقد اجيز فى الناقص ان يكون اعم كاللفظى و هو ما يقصد به تفسیر مدلول اللفظ.
قوله «معرف الشيء»: بعد الفراغ عن بيان ما يتركب منه المعرف شرع فى البحث عنه وقد علمت ان المقصود بالذات في هذا الفن هو البحث عنه وعن الحجة وعرفه بانه ما يحمل على الشيء اى المُعرَّف ليفيد تصور هذا الشيء اما : بكنهه او بوجه يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه و لهذا لم يجز ان يكون اعم لان الاعم لا يفيد شيئاً منهما كالحيوان فى تعريف الانسان فان الحيوان ليس كنه الانسان لان الانسان هو الحيوان الناطق و ايضاً لا يميز الانسان ما عداه لان بعض الحيوان هو الفرس وكذا الحال فى الاعم من وجه واما الاخص اعنى مطلقاً فهو وان جازان يفيد تصوره تصور الاعم بالكنه او بوجه يمتاز عما عداه كما اذا تصورت الانسان بانه حیوان ناطق فقد تصورت فى ضمنه الحيوان باحد الوجهين لكن لما كان الاخص اقل وجوداً في العقل و اخفى فى نظره و شأن المعرف ان يكون اعرف من المعرف لم يجز ان يكون اخص ايضاً وقد علم من تعريف المعرف بما يحمل على الشيء انه لا يجوز ان يكون المعرف مبايناً للمعرف فتعين ان يكون مساو ياله في الصدق. ثم ينبغى ان يكون المعرّف اعرف من المعرّف فى نظر العقل لانه معلوم موصل إلى تصور مجهول هو المعرّف لا اخفى منه ولا مساوياً له في الخفاء والظهور. قوله بالفصل القريب حد التعريف لابد ان يشتمل على امر يخص المعرف ويساويه بناء على ما سبق من اشتراط المساواة فهذا الامر ان كان ذاتياً كان فصلاً قريباً و ان كان عرضياً كان خاصة لا محالة فعلى الاول المعرف حداً وعلى الثانى يسمى رسماً ثم كل منهما ان اشتمل على الجنس القريب يسمى حداً تاماً و رسماً تاماً و ان لم يشتمل على الجنس القريب سواء اشتمل على الجنس البعيد او كان هناك فصل قريب وحده او خاصة وحدها يسمى ناقصاً ورسماً ناقصاً هذا محصل كلامهم وفيه ابحاث لا يسعها المقام.
في المعرف ۵۱ والافناقص و لم يعتبروا با لعرض العام. وقد اجيز فى الناقص ان يكون اعم كاللفظى و هو ما يقصد به تفسیر مدلول اللفظ.
قوله ولم يعتبروا بالعرض العام»: قالوا الغرض من التعريف اما الاطلاع على كنه المعرف او امتيازه عن جميع ما عداه والعرض العام لا يفيد شيئاً منهما فلهذا لم يعتبروه فى مقام التعريف والظاهر ان غرضهم من ذلك أنه لا يعتبر في مقام التعريف انفراداً و اما التعريف بمجموع امور كل واحد منها عرض عالم للمعرف لكن المجموع يخصه كتعريف الانسان بماش مستقيم القامة و تعريف الخفاش بالطائر الولود فهو تعريف بخاصة مركبة معتبرة عندهم كما صرح به بعض المتأخرين.
قوله « وقد اجيز فى الناقص»: اشارة الى ما اجازه المتقدمون حيث حققوا انه يجوز التعريف بالذاتى الاعم كتعريف الانسان بالحيوان فيكون حداً ناقصاً او بالعرض الاعم كتعريفه بالماشى فيكون رسماً ناقصاً بل جوزوا التعريف بالعرض الاخص ايضاً كتعريف الحيوان بالضاحك لكن المصنف لم يعتد به لزعمه انه تعريف بالاخفى وهو غير جايز اصلاً.
قوله «کاللفظی» اى كما اجيز فى التعريف اللفظى ان يكون اعم كقولهم: ((سعد انة نبت» .
قوله «تفسیر مدلول اللفظ» ای تعیین مسمى فى الخاطر فليس فيه تحصيل مجهول من معلوم كما في المعرف الحقيقي فافهم.
Chapter: The definition of a thing is what is said about it to give knowledge of its conceptualization. It is required that it be equal [in scope] and more evident, so it is not valid with a more general or more specific [term], and the equal is a definition, and the more hidden [is not]. And definition by the proximate differentia is a 'hadd', and by the special property [is a 'rasm']; if it is with the proximate genus, it is complete, otherwise incomplete. And they did not consider the common accident. And it has been permitted in the incomplete [definition] that it be more general, like the linguistic, which is intended to interpret the meaning of the word. 
His statement 'the definition of a thing': After finishing the exposition of what the definiens is composed of, he began to research it. And you have known that the essential purpose in this art is the research into it and into the proof. And he defined it as 'what is predicated of the thing, i.e., the definiendum, to give knowledge of the conceptualization of this thing, either by its essence or by a characteristic that distinguishes it from everything else. For this reason, it is not permissible that it be more general, because the more general does not provide either of these, like 'animal' in the definition of 'human'; for 'animal' is not the essence of 'human', because 'human' is 'rational animal', and also it does not distinguish 'human' from other things, because some 'animal' is 'horse'. And similarly for what is more general in some respect. As for the more specific—I mean absolutely—even though it may provide the conceptualization of the more general by its essence or by a characteristic distinguishing it from others, as when you conceptualize 'human' as 'rational animal'—you have thereby conceptualized 'animal' in one of the two ways—nevertheless, because the more specific has less existence in the mind and is more hidden in its view, and the function of the definiens is to be more known than the definiendum, it is not permissible that it be more specific either. And it has been known from the definition of the definiens as 'what is predicated of the thing' that it is not permissible for the definiens to be opposite to the definiendum; so it is determined that it be equal in truth-value. Then it is necessary that the definiens be more known than the definiendum in the view of the mind, because it is a known thing leading to the conceptualization of an unknown thing which is the definiendum, not more hidden than it nor equal to it in hiddenness and manifestness. 
His statement 'by the proximate differentia is a hadd': The definition must include something that is specific to the definiendum and equal to it, based on what preceded of the condition of equality. So that thing, if it is essential, it is a proximate differentia; and if it is accidental, it is necessarily a special property. In the first case, the definiendum is called a 'hadd', and in the second it is called a 'rasm'. Then each of them, if it includes the proximate genus, is called a complete hadd and complete rasm; and if it does not include the proximate genus—whether it includes the remote genus, or there is a proximate differentia alone or a special property alone—it is called an incomplete hadd and incomplete rasm. This is the gist of their statement, and there are discussions that this place cannot accommodate. 
'In the definition' [heading] 51: 'and the incomplete, and they did not consider the common accident. And it has been permitted in the incomplete that it be more general, like the linguistic, which is intended to interpret the meaning of the word.' 
His statement 'and they did not consider the common accident': They said: the purpose of definition is either to attain knowledge of the essence of the definiendum or its distinction from everything else, and the common accident does not provide either of them; therefore they did not consider it in the context of definition. And the apparent meaning is that their purpose is that it is not considered in the context of definition individually. But definition by a set of things each of which is a common accident to the definiendum but the set is specific to it—like the definition of 'human' as 'walker with upright stature' and the definition of 'bat' as 'flying mammal'—that is a definition by a composite special property that is considered valid by them, as stated by some later scholars. 
His statement 'and it has been permitted in the incomplete': This is an indication of what the early scholars permitted, as they verified that it is permissible to define by the more general essential, like defining 'human' by 'animal'—thus it is an incomplete hadd—or by the more general accidental, like defining it by 'walker'—thus it is an incomplete rasm. Rather, they even permitted definition by the more specific accidental, like defining 'animal' by 'risible'. But the author (al-musannif) did not accept it, due to his view that it is a definition by what is more hidden, which is absolutely invalid. 
His statement 'like the linguistic': i.e., as it was permitted in linguistic definition that it be more general, like their saying: 'Sa'danah is a plant.' 
His statement 'interpretation of the meaning of the word': i.e., determining the referent in the mind; so there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known, as in the real definition—so understand.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the definition of definition (ma'rif) itself. It begins by stating that a definition is what is said about a thing to give conceptualization (tasawwur) of it. The author then lays down two conditions: the definition must be equal in scope (musāwin) and more evident (ajlā) than the definiendum. If a definition is broader (a'amm) or narrower (akhass), it fails to convey the essence or distinguish the thing from others. For example, defining 'human' as 'animal' is too broad because 'animal' does not capture the essence of rationality nor distinguish humans from horses. Similarly, defining by a narrower term is problematic because the narrower term is less familiar in the mind and is supposed to be more known than the definiendum, not less. The definition must also be more known than the definiendum; otherwise it cannot lead from the known to the unknown. Then the author distinguishes between two types of definition depending on what specific characteristic (khāṣṣ) is used: if it is an essential differentia (fasl qarīb), it is a hadd (logical definition); if it is an accidental property (khāṣṣa), it is a rasm (description). Each can be complete (tāmm) if it includes the proximate genus (jins qarīb), or incomplete (nāqiṣ) if it lacks the genus or uses a remote genus. Next, the commentator explains why the common accident (al-'araḍ al-'āmm) is not considered in definition—it does not reveal the essence nor uniquely distinguish the definiendum. However, a combination of common accidents that together are specific (like defining humans as 'bipedal walker with upright stature') is acceptable as a composite special property. Finally, the passage notes that early scholars allowed definitions by broader essential or accidental terms in incomplete definitions (e.g., defining human by 'animal' gives an incomplete hadd). They also allowed definition by a narrower accidental term (e.g., defining animal by 'risible'), but the author (likely al-Muṣannif) rejects this because such a term is more hidden than the definiendum, violating the condition of being more evident. The commentary concludes with a note on linguistic definition (tafsīr lafẓī), which can be broader because it only specifies the referent of a word, not providing new knowledge from the unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a complete hadd from an incomplete hadd?
A) The presence of the proximate genus
B) The presence of the proximate differentia
C) The presence of a special property
D) The equality in scope

Key Takeaway
A complete definition includes both the proximate genus and the specific property (essential or accidental); an incomplete definition lacks the genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following definition of 'ijmā'' (consensus) from usul al-fiqh: 'The agreement of the mujtahids of the Muslim community after the death of the Prophet on a religious ruling.' Is it a hadd or rasm? Complete or incomplete? Justify your answer.
?
Create a complete hadd for the term 'qiyās' (analogical reasoning) as defined in usul al-fiqh, and explain why it qualifies as a complete hadd.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Common Accidents and Composite Special Properties
Commentary on common accident, composite properties, and linguistic definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a single common accident cannot define, and how a combination of common accidents can form a composite special property that is valid as a rasm.

Core Idea
A single common accident is not distinctive, but a set of common accidents that together are unique can serve as a definition (composite rasm).

The passage addresses a nuance: common accidents (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) like 'walking' for humans are not adequate definitions individually because they do not reveal the essence or uniquely distinguish. However, if you combine several common accidents that together only apply to the definiendum, the combination functions as a special property (khāṣṣa). This insight allows for definitions that use observable features rather than essential attributes, which is common in fiqh and daily language. Additionally, the lesson covers the permissibility of broader definitions in linguistic (lafẓī) definitions, which merely explain the meaning of a word.

Guided Path
1
Understand why a common accident alone fails
A common accident like 'walking' applies to many beings (humans, animals, etc.). It does not capture the essence (kunnah) nor uniquely distinguish the definiendum. Thus, it is invalid as a single definiens.
The passage says 'they did not consider the common accident' in definition.
2
Recognize composite special properties
If you combine several common accidents that, together, are unique to the definiendum, the combination becomes a special property (khāṣṣa). Example: defining human as 'bipedal walker with upright stature' – each property alone is common, but together they form a composite that uniquely identifies humans.
The commentator mentions this as an acceptable exception, citing 'walker with upright stature' for human and 'flying mammal' for bat.
3
Distinguish between real and linguistic definitions
Linguistic definitions (tafsīr lafẓī) aim only to explain the meaning of a word, not to provide new knowledge of the essence. Therefore, they can be broader than the definiendum, e.g., defining 'sa'dān' as 'a plant' when it is a specific plant.
The passage says it is permitted in incomplete definitions that they be more general, like linguistic definitions.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider defining 'ṣalāt' as 'a set of actions and recitations performed in a specific order with specific intentions'. Here, 'actions' and 'recitations' are common accidents (many acts share these), but the combination is unique. Is this a composite special property? Yes, it functions as a rasm tāmm if the genus 'worship' is included.

It is required that it be equal [in scope] and more evident, so it is not valid with a more general or more specific [term], and the equal is a definition, and the more hidden [is not]. His statement 'and they did not consider the common accident': They said: the purpose of definition is either to attain knowledge of the essence of the definiendum or its distinction from everything else, and the common accident does not provide either of them; therefore they did not consider it in the context of definition. Rather, they even permitted definition by the more specific accidental, like defining 'animal' by 'risible'. But the author (al-musannif) did not accept it, due to his view that it is a definition by what is more hidden, which is absolutely invalid. 
His statement 'like the linguistic': i.e., as it was permitted in linguistic definition that it be more general, like their saying: 'Sa'danah is a plant.' 
His statement 'interpretation of the meaning of the word': i.e., determining the referent in the mind; so there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known, as in the real definition—so understand.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the definition of definition (ma'rif) itself. It begins by stating that a definition is what is said about a thing to give conceptualization (tasawwur) of it. The author then lays down two conditions: the definition must be equal in scope (musāwin) and more evident (ajlā) than the definiendum. If a definition is broader (a'amm) or narrower (akhass), it fails to convey the essence or distinguish the thing from others. For example, defining 'human' as 'animal' is too broad because 'animal' does not capture the essence of rationality nor distinguish humans from horses. Similarly, defining by a narrower term is problematic because the narrower term is less familiar in the mind and is supposed to be more known than the definiendum, not less. The definition must also be more known than the definiendum; otherwise it cannot lead from the known to the unknown. Then the author distinguishes between two types of definition depending on what specific characteristic (khāṣṣ) is used: if it is an essential differentia (fasl qarīb), it is a hadd (logical definition); if it is an accidental property (khāṣṣa), it is a rasm (description). Each can be complete (tāmm) if it includes the proximate genus (jins qarīb), or incomplete (nāqiṣ) if it lacks the genus or uses a remote genus. Next, the commentator explains why the common accident (al-'araḍ al-'āmm) is not considered in definition—it does not reveal the essence nor uniquely distinguish the definiendum. However, a combination of common accidents that together are specific (like defining humans as 'bipedal walker with upright stature') is acceptable as a composite special property. Finally, the passage notes that early scholars allowed definitions by broader essential or accidental terms in incomplete definitions (e.g., defining human by 'animal' gives an incomplete hadd). They also allowed definition by a narrower accidental term (e.g., defining animal by 'risible'), but the author (likely al-Muṣannif) rejects this because such a term is more hidden than the definiendum, violating the condition of being more evident. The commentary concludes with a note on linguistic definition (tafsīr lafẓī), which can be broader because it only specifies the referent of a word, not providing new knowledge from the unknown.

Key Terms
عرض عام
خاصة مركبة
تعريف لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'walker' and 'upright stature' are essential attributes of humans.
B) Because the combination of these common accidents uniquely distinguishes humans from all other things.
C) Because 'walker with upright stature' is a linguistic definition, not a real one.
D) Because the author permits any definition using common accidents.

Key Takeaway
Single common accidents cannot define, but a unique combination of them can serve as a composite special property (rasm), and linguistic definitions are exempt from the equality condition.

Why General Accident is Not a Valid Definition
Commentator explains why general accident is not considered a valid definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given description qualifies as a valid definition by checking if it uses a general accident alone or as part of a compound property.

Core Idea
A general accident fails to define because it doesn't reveal essence or distinguish – but compounded with others, it can.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks why logicians reject the general accident as a definition. He explains the purpose of definition and then clarifies an important exception: while a single general accident is useless, a combination of general accidents that together are unique is actually a valid descriptive definition. This lesson will equip you to apply this rule when evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of definition
The commentator states that definition aims either at revealing the essence (al-ittila' 'ala kunn al-mu'arrif) or at distinguishing the thing from everything else. Keep this dual purpose in mind; any definition must serve at least one.
2
Identify the failure of a single general accident
A general accident (al-'arad al-'amm) is a property shared by multiple species, e.g., 'walking' for humans, birds, etc. It fails both purposes: it doesn't reveal essence because it is accidental, and it doesn't distinguish because it is common. So alone it is rejected.
This is the main point of the passage.
3
Recognize the exception: compound property (al-khassa al-murakkaba)
The commentator clarifies that if multiple general accidents are combined, and the combination is unique to the thing, it becomes a valid definition. Example: defining man as 'upright walking' – 'walking' is general, 'upright' is also general (but together specific). The point is that the whole combination is not shared.
The passage mentions 'māshiyan qā'iman' and 'al-ṭā'ir al-walūd'.
4
Test with an Islamic science example
Take a term like 'sawm' (fasting). A single general accident like 'abstaining from food' is shared by other types of abstention. But the compound 'abstaining from food, drink, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with intention' is unique to the Islamic fasting. Check that this combination is not shared by any other concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولم يعتبروا بالعرض العام: قالوا الغرض من التعريف اما الاطلاع على كنه المعرف او امتيازه عن جميع ما عداه والعرض العام لا يفيد شيئا منهما فلهذا لم يعتبروه في مقام التعريف والظاهر ان غرضهم من ذلك أنه لا يعتبر في مقام التعريف انفرادا و اما التعريف بمجموع امور كل واحد منها عرض عالم للمعرف لكن المجموع يخصه كتعريف الانسان بماش مستقيم القامة و تعريف الخفاش بالطائر الولود فهو تعريف بخاصة مركبة معتبرة عندهم كما صرح به بعض المتاخرين.
His saying: 'And they did not consider the general accident.' They said: The purpose of definition is either to gain insight into the essence of the thing defined or to distinguish it from everything else, and the general accident does not achieve either of these, so they did not consider it in the context of definition. Apparently their intent is that it is not considered individually in the context of definition. As for definition by a combination of matters each of which is a general accident for the thing defined but the whole is specific to it—like defining man as 'upright walking' and defining the bat as 'the flying mammal'—that is a definition by a compound property that is considered valid by them, as some later scholars have stated.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage explains why the general accident ('arad 'amm) is not considered a valid definition. The commentator begins by quoting the matn statement 'and they did not consider the general accident' and then provides the reasoning. The purpose of a definition is either to reveal the essence of the thing defined or to distinguish it from all other things. A general accident fails to do either because it is shared by many things. Therefore, it is not accepted as a definition. However, the commentator clarifies that this rejection applies only when the general accident is used alone. If a collection of general accidents together uniquely identify the thing, such as defining a human as 'upright walking' (both general but together specific) or a bat as 'a flying mammal' (both general but together specific), then such a definition is considered valid. This is called a definition by a compound property (khassa murakkaba), which is accepted by scholars.

Key Terms
العرض العام
الخاصة المركبة
التعريف

B) Because it fails to reveal essence or distinguish the thing
C) Because it is only used in verbal definitions
D) Because it is always false

Key Takeaway
A general accident alone cannot define, but a combination of general accidents that is unique serves as a valid descriptive definition (khassa murakkaba).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Incomplete Definitions: Hadd Naqis and Rasm Naqis
Commentator discusses allowance of broader terms in incomplete definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an incomplete definition (hadd naqis or rasm naqis) and explain why using a more specific accidental is problematic according to some logicians.

Core Idea
An incomplete definition uses a broader term but lacks the specific differentia, sometimes resulting in a description rather than a real definition.

Islamic logicians distinguish between complete and incomplete definitions. Incomplete definitions, like defining man as 'animal', capture the genus but omit the differentia. This lesson explores what makes a definition incomplete and why some scholars reject certain types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. In the passage, examples include 'man' (الإنسان) and 'animal' (الحيوان).
2
Extract the genus and differentia
In a hadd, the genus is given but the differentia is missing. For instance, 'animal' is the genus for man, but the differentia 'rational' is omitted, making it an incomplete definition.
3
Classify the definition type
Determine if it is a hadd naqis (using only genus) or rasm naqis (using an accidental property). For example, defining man as 'walking' is a rasm naqis because walking is an accidental property.
4
Apply the author's objection
The author (al-musannif) rejects definition by a more specific accidental (العرض الأخص) like defining animal as 'laughing', arguing it defines by what is more obscure, which is invalid.
The earlier scholars permitted it, but the author did not consider it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وقد اجيز في الناقص: اشارة الى ما اجازه المتقدمون حيث حققوا انه يجوز التعريف بالذاتي الاعم كتعريف الانسان بالحيوان فيكون حدا ناقصا او بالعرض الاعم كتعريفه بالماشي فيكون رسما ناقصا بل جوزوا التعريف بالعرض الاخص ايضا كتعريف الحيوان بالضاحك لكن المصنف لم يعتد به لزعمه انه تعريف بالاخفى وهو غير جايز اصلا. قوله كاللفظي اى كما اجيز في التعريف اللفظي ان يكون اعم كقولهم: سعد انة نبت. اللفظ من بين المعانى المخزونة قوله تفسير مدلول اللفظ ای تعيين مسمى في الخاطر فليس فيه تحصيل مجهول من معلوم كما في المعرف الحقيقي فافهم.
His saying: 'And it has been permitted in the incomplete [definition]' is a reference to what the earlier scholars permitted, for they verified that it is permissible to define by a more general essential, like defining man as animal, resulting in an incomplete definition; or by a more general accidental, like defining him as walking, resulting in an incomplete description. Rather, they even permitted definition by a more specific accidental, like defining animal as laughing. But the author did not consider this, because he claimed it is a definition by what is more obscure, which is not permissible at all. His saying: 'like the verbal' i.e., just as it is permitted in verbal definition to be more general, as in their saying: 'Sa'd is a plant.' (The word [Sa'd] is among the stored meanings). His saying: 'interpretation of the meaning of the word' i.e., specifying the named in the mind; there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known in it, as in real definition. So understand.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage discusses two types of definitions: incomplete definitions (had naqis, rasm naqis) and verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi). The commentator explains that earlier scholars allowed using a broader essential term in definition, e.g., defining man as 'animal' (an incomplete definition), or a broader accidental term like 'walking' (an incomplete description). They even allowed a more specific accidental, e.g., defining animal as 'laughing'. However, the author (al-musannif) rejected this last type, arguing that it defines something known by something more obscure, which is invalid. The passage then turns to verbal definition, which is allowed to be more general, like explaining the word 'Sa'd' by saying it is a plant (i.e., a kind of grass). Verbal definition simply clarifies the meaning of a word in the mind; it does not produce new knowledge (tas'il majhul min ma'lum) as real definitions do. The commentator concludes by urging the reader to understand this distinction carefully.

Key Terms
الحد الناقص
الرسم الناقص
العرض الاخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Defining human as 'rational animal'
B) Defining human as 'animal'
C) Defining human as 'walking'
D) Defining human as 'laughing'

Key Takeaway
Incomplete definitions use broader essential or accidental terms and are acceptable when the specific differentia is unknown or unnecessary, but they do not capture the full essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Verbal Definition: Ta'rif Lafzi
Commentator discusses verbal definition as a clarification of word meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) from a real definition (hadd haqiqi) and explain its purpose in clarifying word meanings.

Core Idea
Verbal definition explains the meaning of a word, not the essence of a thing; it provides no new knowledge about reality.

Not all definitions aim to reveal essence. Verbal definitions simply clarify what a word means, often using synonyms or broader phrases. This lesson examines how verbal definitions function and why they are not considered real definitions.

Guided Path
1
Look for a word being explained
Identify the word that is being defined. In the passage, the word 'Sa'd' is defined as 'a plant'.
2
Check if it uses a synonym or broader category
Verbal definitions often use a more general term or a synonym. 'Sa'd' is a type of plant, so the definition uses a broader category.
3
Determine if it adds knowledge
The commentator says verbal definition 'specifies the named in the mind; there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known'. It only clarifies the referent, not the essence.
Contrast with real definitions (hadd haqiqi) which proceed from known to unknown.
4
Contrast with real definition
Real definitions involve genus and differentia and provide new knowledge about the essence. Verbal definitions are mere linguistic clarifications.
Example: Defining 'salat' as 'prayer' is verbal; defining it as 'specific actions and words starting with takbir and ending with taslim' is real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وقد اجيز في الناقص: اشارة الى ما اجازه المتقدمون حيث حققوا انه يجوز التعريف بالذاتي الاعم كتعريف الانسان بالحيوان فيكون حدا ناقصا او بالعرض الاعم كتعريفه بالماشي فيكون رسما ناقصا بل جوزوا التعريف بالعرض الاخص ايضا كتعريف الحيوان بالضاحك لكن المصنف لم يعتد به لزعمه انه تعريف بالاخفى وهو غير جايز اصلا. قوله كاللفظي اى كما اجيز في التعريف اللفظي ان يكون اعم كقولهم: سعد انة نبت. اللفظ من بين المعانى المخزونة قوله تفسير مدلول اللفظ ای تعيين مسمى في الخاطر فليس فيه تحصيل مجهول من معلوم كما في المعرف الحقيقي فافهم.
His saying: 'And it has been permitted in the incomplete [definition]' is a reference to what the earlier scholars permitted, for they verified that it is permissible to define by a more general essential, like defining man as animal, resulting in an incomplete definition; or by a more general accidental, like defining him as walking, resulting in an incomplete description. His saying: 'interpretation of the meaning of the word' i.e., specifying the named in the mind; there is no acquisition of an unknown from a known in it, as in real definition. So understand.

Essential vs. Accidental Disjunctions
Commentator distinguishes disjunctions based on essential vs accidental opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential ('anādiyya) from accidental (ittifāqiyya) disjunctions and identify examples of each in Islamic science contexts.

Core Idea
Essential opposition is inherent; accidental opposition depends on context.

In this lesson, we explore a key classification of disjunctive propositions: whether the opposition between the two parts is essential or accidental. This distinction affects the strength of the disjunction in logical arguments. The commentator clarifies the author's division and provides examples that anchor the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
From the passage, find the terms 'anādiyya (essential) and ittifāqiyya (accidental). Note that some also call them luzūmiyya and ittifāqiyya respectively.
2
Understand the criterion
The key is whether the opposition arises from the essences of the parts (dhātī) or from the particular subject matter (mādda). Essential opposition holds in any context; accidental depends on context.
Dhātī here means inherent to the parts themselves, not accidental.
3
Examine the examples
Essential: even vs odd – in any number, they are opposite. Accidental: blackness vs writing in a person – a person can be both black and a writer; the opposition is not inherent.
The commentator uses these exact examples from the source.
4
Apply to Islamic science
Think of a disjunction in a fiqh, usul, or nahw context. Does the opposition hold in all cases or only in specific circumstances?
We will apply this in the application section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المصنف: وكل منها عنادية... إنما سميت بها لاشتمالها على التنافي والعناد بين الجزئين، وما ذكر في اللزومية يأتي هنا أيضاً سؤالاً وجواباً. ومنهم من يسميها أيضاً لزومية كما سمى مقابلها اتفاقية، ولعل نظره إلى لزوم أحد المعاندين لعين الآخر ولزوم عينه لنقيض الآخر، ولا مشاقة في الاصطلاح. ثم اعلم أن كلام المصنف هنا أيضاً ظاهر في انحصار المنفصلة في العنادية والاتفاقية كما هو ظاهر. ثلث: ومنهم من التقسيم هنا أيضاً قال: فإن اكتفى بمطلق التنافي سميت منفصلة مطلقة، وإن قيد للذاتي الجزئين فالعاتدية، والافاتفاقية. قوله «لذاتي الجزئين» أي إن المنا‌فاة بين الطرفين أي المقدم والتالي منا‌فاة ناشئة عن ذاتيهما في أي مادة تحققا، كالمنا‌فاة بين الزوجية والفردية، لا عن خصوص المادة كالمنا‌فاة بين السواد والكتابة في إنسان يكون أسود وكاتباً. التنافي بكونه ذاتياً، سميت منفصلة عنادية، وإن قيد بالاتفاق سميت منفصلة اتفاقية. هذا. وعلى ما اختاره المصنف يندرج المطلقة تحت الاتفاقية فافهم.
The author's statement: 'And each of them is antagonistic...' It is only named such for its inclusion of opposition and antagonism between the two parts. What was mentioned about the implicative will come here also as a question and answer. Some also call it implicative just as its opposite is called accidental, and perhaps his view is the necessary consequence of one of the two antagonists for the very other and the necessary consequence of its very self for the opposite of the other, and there is no difficulty in terminology. Then know that the author's speech here is also apparent in the restriction of the disjunctive to the antagonistic and the accidental, as is clear. And some also said about the division here: If it suffices with absolute opposition, it is called an absolute disjunctive, and if it is restricted to the essential for the two parts, then it is antagonistic, and the accidental. His statement: 'for the essential of the two parts' means that the opposition between the two sides, i.e., the antecedent and consequent, is an opposition arising from their essences in any matter they are realized, like the opposition between evenness and oddness, not from the particular matter, like the opposition between blackness and writing in a person who is black and a writer. The opposition, by being essential, is called an antagonistic disjunctive, and if restricted to the accidental, it is called an accidental disjunctive. This is it. And according to what the author chose, the absolute falls under the accidental, so understand.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two kinds of disjunctive propositions based on the nature of the opposition between their parts. The author introduces the term 'anadiyya (essential opposition) and ittifaqiyya (accidental opposition). The commentator explains that some scholars also call the essential disjunction 'luzumiyya' (implicative) because one part necessitates the opposite of the other. The key distinction is whether the opposition arises from the essences of the two parts (dhātī) or from a particular matter (mādda). Essential opposition is exemplified by the pair even-odd: in any number, one of these is true and they cannot both be true, regardless of context. Accidental opposition is exemplified by blackness and writing in a person: it is possible for someone to be both black and a writer, or neither; the opposition is not inherent. The commentator further notes that some scholars add a third type: 'absolute disjunction' (munfasila muṭlaqa), which considers opposition in an unrestricted sense. According to the author's choice, this absolute type is included under accidental opposition. This classification is important for evaluating the strength of disjunctive statements in arguments.

Key Terms
عنادية
اتفاقية
ذاتي
مادة
مطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an essential (ʻanādiyya) disjunction?
A) Either this person is black or he is a writer.
B) Either this number is even or it is odd.
C) Either this liquid is water or it is oil.
D) Either this animal is a horse or it is black.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctions are classified as essential ('anādiyya) when opposition is inherent, and accidental (ittifāqiyya) when it depends on the subject matter; absolute disjunctions fall under accidental according to this author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Method of Tardid for Identifying the Cause
Author defines the method of tardid for identifying the effective cause of a ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tardid method to determine the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling in analogical reasoning (qiyas).

Core Idea
Tardid systematically eliminates all possible causes until only the true cause remains.

In usul al-fiqh, identifying the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling is essential for analogical reasoning (qiyas). How can a jurist be sure that a particular attribute is indeed the cause? The method of tardid (also called sabr and taqsim) provides a systematic procedure: list all conceivable attributes of the original case (asl), then eliminate each by finding a counterexample where the attribute exists without the ruling. The remaining attribute is the cause. This lesson unpacks this method using the classic example of wine prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original case and its ruling
Start with the original case (asl) whose ruling is known. For example, wine (khamr) is prohibited. Understand the ruling to be extended to new cases via qiyas.
2
List all possible attributes that could be the cause
Enumerate every attribute of the original case that might plausibly be the cause of the ruling. In the wine example: being made from grapes, being a liquid, specific color, taste, smell, and intoxication.
The list should be exhaustive of reasonable candidates.
3
Eliminate each candidate using counterexamples
For each candidate attribute, find a case where that attribute exists but the ruling does not. If such a counterexample exists, the candidate cannot be the cause. For instance, date syrup (dibs) is made from grapes but is not prohibited, so 'made from grapes' is eliminated.
Continue until only one candidate remains.
4
Confirm the remaining attribute as the cause
The attribute that survives all eliminations is the effective cause ('illa). In the example, intoxication remains after eliminating all others, so intoxication is the cause of prohibition.
This attribute can now be used to extend the ruling to other intoxicants via qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: الترديد و يسمى بـ «السبر» و «التقسيم» ايضاً و هو ان يتفحص اولا بـ اوصاف الاصل و يردد ان علة الحكم هل هى هذه الصفة او تلك؟ ثم يبطل ثانياً حكم علية كلّ كلّ حتى يستقر على وصف واحد و يستفاد من ذلك كون هذا الوصف علة كما يقال علة حرمة الخمر اما الاتخاذ من العنب او الميعان او اللون المخصوص او الطعم المخصوص او الرائحة المخصوصة او الاسكار لكن الاول ليس بعلة لوجوده فى الديس بدون الحرمة و كذا البواقى ماسوى الاسكار يمثل ما ذكر فتعين الاسكار للعلية .
The second: al-tardid (disjunction), also called al-sabr (testing) and al-taqsim (division). It is to first examine the attributes of the original (case) and to alternate [saying] whether the cause of the ruling is this attribute or that. Then, secondly, to invalidate the ruling of causality for each one until it settles on one attribute, and from that it is understood that this attribute is the cause. As it is said: the cause of the prohibition of wine is either it being taken from grapes, or being liquid, or the specific color, or the specific taste, or the specific smell, or intoxication. But the first is not a cause because it exists in date syrup without prohibition, and likewise the rest except for intoxication—as mentioned—so intoxication is determined for causality.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines a method used in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) to identify the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling. The method is called tardid (also sabr and taqsim). It involves two steps: first, listing all possible attributes of the original case (asl) that could be the cause; second, eliminating each candidate until one remains. The remaining attribute is then taken as the cause. The author illustrates with the classic example of wine (khamr) prohibition. The possible causes considered include: being made from grapes, being liquid, specific color, taste, smell, and intoxication. Each is eliminated except intoxication. For instance, being made from grapes is not the cause because date syrup (also from grapes) is not prohibited. Thus intoxication is established as the cause. This method is foundational for analogical reasoning (qiyas) in Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
الترديد
السبر
التقسيم
العلة

C) Because the color of wine is different from grapes.
D) Because intoxication is the only remaining attribute.

Key Takeaway
Tardid is a systematic elimination method used in usul al-fiqh to identify the effective cause of a ruling by disjunctively enumerating possible attributes and eliminating all but one through counterexamples.

Classification of Predicates in Sciences
Author classifies predicates of scientific propositions into three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any predicate in a scientific proposition as type, essential accident, or composite, and identify the components of a composite predicate.

Core Idea
Scientific predicates are either the subject's type, its essential accident, or both combined.

In the Islamic sciences, the logical structure of propositions matters. This passage teaches us how to examine the relationship between subject and predicate in demonstrative statements. By mastering this classification, you will be able to analyze definitions and descriptions in fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of predicates
The author states that predicates in the sciences are either: 1) a type (naw') of the subject, 2) an essential accident ('arad dhati) of the subject, or 3) composite (murakkab). Write these three categories.
2
Understand 'type' (naw') as predicate
When the predicate is the genus or species of the subject, it is called a type. For example, 'Man is an animal' – 'animal' is the genus of 'man'. In the source, the author does not give an example here, but we can infer from the logic tradition.
The subject is a species of the predicate.
3
Examine 'essential accident' predicate
An essential accident is a property that necessarily belongs to the subject, like 'Every moving thing has inclination'. The predicate 'has inclination' is not the essence of a moving thing but necessarily follows from it. Identify that the predicate is an accident inseparable from the subject.
The example comes from physics (natural philosophy).
4
Analyze composite predicates
Composite predicates combine the subject (or its type) with an essential accident. The author gives two subcases: (a) subject + essential accident, e.g., 'Every magnitude that is a mean proportional is a side of the rectangle contained by the extremes'; (b) type of subject + essential accident, e.g., 'Every line that stands on a line makes its adjacent angles right or equal'. Break down each example into subject/type and essential accident.
These are from geometry, a demonstrative science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أجزاء العلوم نوع منه أو عرض ذاتي له أو مركب و محمولاتها أمور خارجة عنها لاحقة لها لذواتها. قوله أو عرض ذاتي له كقولهم: كل متحرك فله ميل. قوله أو مركب من الموضوع مع العرض الذاتي كقول المهندس: كل مقدار وسط في النسبة فهو ضلع ما يحيط به الطرفان أو من نوعه مع العرض الذاتي كقوله: كل خط قام على خط فان زاويتي جنبيه قائمتان أو متساويتان.
In the parts of the sciences: it is either a type of it, or an essential accident of it, or composite; and their predicates are extraneous matters consequent upon them for their essences. He says: 'Or an essential accident of it', like their saying: 'Every moving thing has an inclination'. He says: 'Or composite from the subject with the essential accident', like the geometer's saying: 'Every magnitude that is a middle in proportion is a side of what is enclosed by the two extremes'; or from its type with the essential accident, like his saying: 'Every line that stands upon a line, then its two adjacent angles are right or equal.'
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines the possible relations of predicates to subjects in the sciences. The author first states that in the parts of sciences, the predicate is either a type of the subject (i.e., the subject is a species of the predicate), or an essential accident of it, or a composite. The composite can be either the subject plus its essential accident, or the type of the subject plus its essential accident. Examples are given: for essential accident, 'Every moving thing has an inclination' (from physics). For composite, the geometer's example: 'Every magnitude that is a mean proportional is a side of the rectangle contained by the extremes' is a composite of subject (magnitude) and essential accident (being mean). The other example is from geometry: 'Every line that stands on a line makes its adjacent angles right or equal'—here the subject is 'line' (which is a type of magnitude) and the predicate includes the essential accident of that type. These examples illustrate how predicates in demonstrative sciences relate to their subjects.

Key Takeaway
