Detecting Circular Definitions in Logic
Commentator identifies and explains a potential circular definition of qadiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a circular definition in a logical term and explain why it is invalid using the example of qadiyya.

Core Idea
A circular definition makes the definiendum depend on itself, rendering it useless.

In this passage, the commentator warns against a subtle error: defining a proposition (qadiyya) in a way that assumes the very concept being defined. Because qadiyya is essentially a statement, defining it as 'a statement that can be truthful or false' using 'truthful' in the sense of 'a truthful statement' creates a circle. We will walk through the objection to see exactly how circularity arises and why it must be avoided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
The commentator cites the definition: 'qadiyya is a statement that can be truthful or false.' Note the key terms: qadiyya (proposition), sidq (truth), kidhb (falsehood).
2
Clarify the Meanings of Sidq and Kidhb
If sidq means 'a truthful statement' and kidhb means 'a false statement', then the definition becomes: 'a statement that can be a truthful statement or a false statement.'
3
Recognize Synonymy
Qadiyya itself means 'statement'. So the definiens repeats the definiendum: qadiyya is defined as 'a statement that can be a truthful statement or a false statement', essentially saying 'a statement is a statement that can be truthful or false'.
4
Understand the Consequence
Circular definitions imply self-dependence: the thing is defined in terms of itself. This violates the requirement that a definition should clarify the meaning, not assume it. The commentator declares this invalid because 'it entails the thing depending on itself'.
This is a foundational principle in logic: the definiendum must not appear in the definiens in any form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و قد يطلق الصدق و الحق الغرض من هذا الكلام دفع مارتها يتوهمه في هذا المقام من ليس له تحقيق من الانام و هو ان المفهوم من الكلام السابق ان الصدق هو الخبر المطابق للمواقع ويعلم هذا بالمقابلة ان الكذب هو الخبر الغير المطابق وقلتم فى تعريف القضية فيما بعد : ان القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب ومعناه على التفسير المذكور القضية قول يحتمل الخبر المطابق و الخبر الغير المطابق فحينئذ يلزم اخذ المعرف (بفتح الراء) في المعرف ( بالكسر) فان القضية والخير مترادفان و هو باطل لاستلزامه توقف الشيء على نفسه و بطلانه واضح وسيأتي وتقرير :الجواب: ان الصدق كما يطلق على الخبر المطابق كذلك يطلق على نفس المطابقية وكذلك الكذب ايضاً يطلق على نفس اللامطابقية وهذا المعنى هو المراد من قولنا : «القضية قول ... فحينئذ يرتفع الاشكال. و لقائل ان يقول : فحينئذ يلزم استعمال اللفظ المشترك في التعريف و هو غير جيد كما سيأتي . وقد يجاب ان الصدق والكذب بهذا المعنى حقيقة ، لانه المعنى المصدرى دون المعنى الآخر فانه مجاز : من قبيل اطلاق المصدر على الصفة نحو «زيد عدل» على أحد الوجوه السابقة ولا يذهب الى المجاز مع وجود الحقيقة. و فيه اولاً : انا لانسلم انها بالمعنى المذكور حقيقة فان الصدق مثلاً كما سبق هو الاخبار عن الشيء على ما هو عليه وهو صفة المتكلم فيكون استعماله في المعنى المذكور ايضاً مجازاً و هكذا الكذب. وثانياً: ان ذلك المعنى و ان كان معنى مجازياً ، الا انه اكثر استعماله فيما بين القوم بحيث يفهم منها ذلك المعنى عند الاطلاق من غير قرينة كما هو ظاهر لمن تتبع مستعملات الاقوام. فالحق في الجواب ان يقال: ان ذلك المعنى المجازى و ان بلغ يسبب كثرته مرتبة الحقيقة، الا ان عدم صحة معنى التعريف على ذلك يدل على عدم ارادته فافهم (محمد على)
His statement: 'and sidq and haqq may be applied'. The purpose of this speech is to repel what may be imagined by one who has no investigation among people, namely that the understanding from the previous speech is that sidq is the statement corresponding to reality, and by contrast one knows that falsehood is the statement not corresponding. And you said later in the definition of qadiyya that qadiyya is a statement that can be sidq or falsehood; its meaning according to the mentioned interpretation is that qadiyya is a statement that can be the corresponding statement or the non-corresponding statement. Then it would entail taking the definiendum in the definiens, for qadiyya and statement are synonymous, and that is invalid because it entails the thing depending on itself, and its invalidity is clear. And it will come later. The answer: sidq, just as it is applied to the corresponding statement, is also applied to correspondence itself; likewise falsehood is applied to non-correspondence itself. And this meaning is intended by our saying: 'qadiyya is a statement...' Then the problem is removed. And one may object: then it would entail using a homonym in the definition, which is not good as will come later. And it may be answered that sidq and falsehood in this meaning are literal, because it is the verbal noun meaning rather than the other meaning, for that is metaphorical: like applying the verbal noun to the attribute as in 'Zayd is justice' according to one of the previous ways, and one does not resort to metaphor when the literal exists. But there is in it first: we do not concede that they are literal in the mentioned meaning, for sidq, for example, as before, is reporting about a thing as it is, and it is an attribute of the speaker, so its use in the mentioned meaning is also metaphorical, and likewise falsehood. Second: that meaning, even if it is metaphorical, however it is more frequently used among the people such that that meaning is understood upon absolute usage without a context, as is apparent to one who examines the usages of people. So the correct answer is to say: that metaphorical meaning, even if due to its frequency it reaches the level of the literal, the fact that the meaning of the definition is not valid upon it indicates that it is not intended. So reflect (Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential circularity in the definition of qadiyya (proposition). The objection is that if qadiyya is defined as 'a statement that can be truthful or false', and if truthful means 'corresponding to reality' and false means 'non-corresponding', then the definition essentially repeats the word 'statement', creating circularity. The proposed solution is that sidq and falsehood can be taken as abstract nouns meaning 'correspondence' and 'non-correspondence', not as predicates of the statement. Thus 'a statement that can be correspondence or non-correspondence' is not circular. However, this raises the issue of using a homonym (since sidq has multiple meanings). The commentator then debates whether the abstract meaning is literal or metaphorical, concluding that even if metaphorical, its frequent use gives it a quasi-literal status. Ultimately, the definition's validity determines which meaning is intended.

Key Terms
القضية
الصدق
الكذب

Checkpoint

C) Because it is too broad.
D) Because the definition is metaphysical.

Key Takeaway
A definition must not include the term being defined or its synonym in the definiens, as this creates logical circularity.

Resolving Circularity Using Abstract Meanings
Commentator resolves circular definition by using abstract meanings of sidq and kidhb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve an apparent circular definition by shifting to abstract meanings of key terms, as demonstrated with sidq and kidhb.

Core Idea
Abstract meanings of truth and falsehood break the circularity without changing the definition's form.

After identifying the circularity in defining qadiyya as 'a statement that can be truthful or false', the commentator provides a solution: interpret sidq and kidhb as abstract correspondence and non-correspondence, not as attributes of the statement. This subtle shift removes the synonymy because the definiens now speaks of a statement that can possess correspondence or non-correspondence, not a statement that is a truthful statement. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Circular Definition
The problematic definition: 'qadiyya is a statement that can be sidq or kidhb', where sidq = truthful statement, kidhb = false statement. This is circular because qadiyya = statement.
2
Introduce the Abstract Meanings
The commentator notes that sidq can also mean 'correspondence' (al-mutabaqiyya) itself, and kidhb can mean 'non-correspondence' (adam al-mutabaqa). These are abstract nouns, not adjectives.
In Arabic, sidq is a masdar (verbal noun) that can be used concretely or abstractly.
3
Apply the Abstract Meanings to the Definition
Now the definition reads: 'qadiyya is a statement that can be correspondence or non-correspondence'. This is not circular because 'statement' and 'correspondence' are different concepts.
4
Check that the Definition Works
The definition now makes sense: a proposition is a statement that can be evaluated as either corresponding to reality (true) or not (false). The circularity disappears, validating the definition.
This shows how shifting from concrete to abstract meanings can salvage a definition.

قوله و قد يطلق الصدق و الحق الغرض من هذا الكلام دفع مارتها يتوهمه في هذا المقام من ليس له تحقيق من الانام و هو ان المفهوم من الكلام السابق ان الصدق هو الخبر المطابق للمواقع ويعلم هذا بالمقابلة ان الكذب هو الخبر الغير المطابق وقلتم فى تعريف القضية فيما بعد : ان القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب ومعناه على التفسير المذكور القضية قول يحتمل الخبر المطابق و الخبر الغير المطابق فحينئذ يلزم اخذ المعرف (بفتح الراء) في المعرف ( بالكسر) فان القضية والخير مترادفان و هو باطل لاستلزامه توقف الشيء على نفسه و بطلانه واضح وسيأتي وتقرير :الجواب: ان الصدق كما يطلق على الخبر المطابق كذلك يطلق على نفس المطابقية وكذلك الكذب ايضاً يطلق على نفس اللامطابقية وهذا المعنى هو المراد من قولنا : «القضية قول ... فحينئذ يرتفع الاشكال. و لقائل ان يقول : فحينئذ يلزم استعمال اللفظ المشترك في التعريف و هو غير جيد كما سيأتي . وقد يجاب ان الصدق والكذب بهذا المعنى حقيقة ، لانه المعنى المصدرى دون المعنى الآخر فانه مجاز : من قبيل اطلاق المصدر على الصفة نحو «زيد عدل» على أحد الوجوه السابقة ولا يذهب الى المجاز مع وجود الحقيقة. و فيه اولاً : انا لانسلم انها بالمعنى المذكور حقيقة فان الصدق مثلاً كما سبق هو الاخبار عن الشيء على ما هو عليه وهو صفة المتكلم فيكون استعماله في المعنى المذكور ايضاً مجازاً و هكذا الكذب. وثانياً: ان ذلك المعنى و ان كان معنى مجازياً ، الا انه اكثر استعماله فيما بين القوم بحيث يفهم منها ذلك المعنى عند الاطلاق من غير قرينة كما هو ظاهر لمن تتبع مستعملات الاقوام. فالحق في الجواب ان يقال: ان ذلك المعنى المجازى و ان بلغ يسبب كثرته مرتبة الحقيقة، الا ان عدم صحة معنى التعريف على ذلك يدل على عدم ارادته فافهم (محمد على)
His statement: 'and sidq and haqq may be applied'. The purpose of this speech is to repel what may be imagined by one who has no investigation among people, namely that the understanding from the previous speech is that sidq is the statement corresponding to reality, and by contrast one knows that falsehood is the statement not corresponding. And you said later in the definition of qadiyya that qadiyya is a statement that can be sidq or falsehood; its meaning according to the mentioned interpretation is that qadiyya is a statement that can be the corresponding statement or the non-corresponding statement. Then it would entail taking the definiendum in the definiens, for qadiyya and statement are synonymous, and that is invalid because it entails the thing depending on itself, and its invalidity is clear. And it will come later. The answer: sidq, just as it is applied to the corresponding statement, is also applied to correspondence itself; likewise falsehood is applied to non-correspondence itself. And this meaning is intended by our saying: 'qadiyya is a statement...' Then the problem is removed. And one may object: then it would entail using a homonym in the definition, which is not good as will come later. And it may be answered that sidq and falsehood in this meaning are literal, because it is the verbal noun meaning rather than the other meaning, for that is metaphorical: like applying the verbal noun to the attribute as in 'Zayd is justice' according to one of the previous ways, and one does not resort to metaphor when the literal exists. But there is in it first: we do not concede that they are literal in the mentioned meaning, for sidq, for example, as before, is reporting about a thing as it is, and it is an attribute of the speaker, so its use in the mentioned meaning is also metaphorical, and likewise falsehood. Second: that meaning, even if it is metaphorical, however it is more frequently used among the people such that that meaning is understood upon absolute usage without a context, as is apparent to one who examines the usages of people. So the correct answer is to say: that metaphorical meaning, even if due to its frequency it reaches the level of the literal, the fact that the meaning of the definition is not valid upon it indicates that it is not intended. So reflect (Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential circularity in the definition of qadiyya (proposition). The objection is that if qadiyya is defined as 'a statement that can be truthful or false', and if truthful means 'corresponding to reality' and false means 'non-corresponding', then the definition essentially repeats the word 'statement', creating circularity. The proposed solution is that sidq and falsehood can be taken as abstract nouns meaning 'correspondence' and 'non-correspondence', not as predicates of the statement. Thus 'a statement that can be correspondence or non-correspondence' is not circular. However, this raises the issue of using a homonym (since sidq has multiple meanings). The commentator then debates whether the abstract meaning is literal or metaphorical, concluding that even if metaphorical, its frequent use gives it a quasi-literal status. Ultimately, the definition's validity determines which meaning is intended.

الصدق
الكذب

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the circularity in the definition of qadiyya?
A) By changing the genus of the definition.
B) By interpreting sidq and kidhb as abstract correspondence and non-correspondence.
C) By adding more terms to the definition.
D) By denying that the definition is circular.

Key Takeaway
Shifting from concrete to abstract meanings of key terms can eliminate circularity without altering the definition's structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the definition: 'A valid syllogism is an argument that produces certain knowledge.' Could this be circular if 'knowledge' is defined using 'syllogism'? Revise the definition using abstract terms.
?
2. In fiqh, 'sunna' is defined as 'an action that is rewarded but not punished if left'. Is this circular? Explain how abstract meanings of reward and punishment avoid circularity.

Handling Homonymy in Definitions
Commentator addresses the homonymy objection and concludes definition validity determines intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the use of homonymous terms in definitions and apply the principle that the intended meaning is determined by the definition's validity.

Core Idea
A homonym in a definition is acceptable if the intended meaning is clear, and the definition's success indicates which meaning is intended.

After resolving the circularity, a new objection arises: using words with multiple meanings (homonyms) in definitions is problematic. The commentator engages in a detailed discussion about whether the abstract meanings of sidq and kidhb are literal or metaphorical, and whether using a homonymous term is permissible. He eventually concludes that the very fact that the definition works (is not circular) with the abstract meaning shows that this is the intended meaning, regardless of literal/metaphorical status. Let's analyze this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Homonymy Objection
The objector says: 'If sidq means both truthful statement and correspondence, then using it in the definition introduces ambiguity. This is not good practice in definitions.'
Homonymous terms can cause confusion if the intended sense is not clear.
2
Examine the Literal/Metaphorical Debate
The commentator first considers the claim that the abstract meaning is literal (the masdar meaning) and the concrete meaning is metaphorical. He refutes this by arguing that sidq is originally a quality of the speaker, making the concrete meaning more literal. Thus the abstract meaning is metaphorical.
This debate is important for understanding Arabic semantics.
3
Consider Frequency of Usage
Even if metaphorical, the abstract meaning is very frequently used among scholars, so it becomes understood without context (haqiqa 'urfiya). Still, the definition's validity is key.
4
Apply the Principle: Definition Determines Intended Meaning
The commentator's final answer: 'If the meaning of the definition is not valid upon that [other] meaning, it indicates that it is not intended.' In other words, because the circular interpretation leads to an invalid definition, and the abstract interpretation yields a valid definition, the abstract meaning must be intended.
This is a pragmatic criterion: the purpose of the definition guides which meaning is operative.

Distinguishing Zarf Mustaqir and Zarf Laghw
Definition of mustaqir and laghw, two definitions, and defense of substantive difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish zarf mustaqir from zarf laghw based on the two definitions and determine whether a given adverbial phrase is mustaqir or laghw by analyzing the governing verb.

Core Idea
The dispute over mustaqir versus laghw is not mere terminology—it affects how every adverbial phrase in Arabic is interpreted.

In this lesson, we explore how Arabic grammarians define two types of adverbial constructions: mustaqir and laghw. The passage presents two definitions, the second hinging on whether the governing verb is general or specific. The author defends the substantive nature of this distinction, showing that the choice of definition impacts how we parse sentences, especially in Qur'anic exegesis and fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the first definition (based on pronoun transfer into the deleted muta'allaq) and the second definition (based on the factor being a general verb like kāna vs. a specific verb like rakiba).
The first definition appears in the opening lines; the second is introduced with 'that said, it has been said...'
2
Understand the criterion for mustaqir
In the second definition, a zarf mustaqir has a governing verb that is general, indicating absolute existence. A zarf laghw has a specific verb, even if the verb is deleted. Note that the muhaqqiqun prefer the first definition.
General verbs include kawn, wujud, husul.
3
Analyse al-Sharif's elaboration
Al-Sharif explains that in mustaqir, the muta'allaq is deleted but understood; even if a specific verb is implied, the general verb can be estimated for i'rab. However, the specific verb still settles in the zarf.
Examples: 'Zayd is on the horse' implies 'riding', but kāna is estimated for grammar.
4
Evaluate the dispute
Read the author's argument that the difference is substantive. He provides the example 'Man laka bi-l-muhadhdhab' where only a specific verb (yadman) can be estimated, proving that the two definitions are not mere verbal variations.
This step refutes the possibility that the dispute is just terminological.
5
Apply the distinction
Given an Arabic sentence, decide: Is the adverbial mustaqir or laghw? Check whether a general verb suffices or a specific verb is required. Use al-Sharif's principle that mustaqir allows estimation of a general verb for i'rab.
Practice with sentences containing zarf like 'zaydun fī d-dāri' vs. 'man laka bi-l-muhadhdhab'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما فى الثانى اى كما ان فى الثانى يستقر على ما سيجيء والثاني مستقراً، لاستقرار ضمير متعلقه فيه لان في نحو «زيد فى الدار» مثلاً لما حذف المتعلق انتقل ضميره الى فى الدار فاستقر فيه فهو «مستقرفيه» لكنهم يقولون فيه المستقر» كما يقولون فى «المشترك فيه» «المشترك». او لانه يفهم منه معنى عامله المحذوف فكانه مستقر فيه. والمستقر بهذا المعنى ايضاً من باب حذف الصلة. ذلك و قد يقال : ان الظرف المستقر ما يكون عامله من الافعال العامة التى لا يخلو عنها فعل من نحو الكون والوجود والحصول وغير ذلك مما يدل على كون مطلق واللغو ما يكون متعلقه من الافعال الخاصة التي تدل على كون مخصوص حذف ام لم يحذف. والمحققون على الاول. قال المحقق الشريف في حاشية الكشاف ان الظرف المستقر عندهم ما لم يذكر متعلقه و فهم منه فكان المتعلق مستقرفيه فان لم يفهم منه سوى الأفعال العامة كان المقدر منها وان فهم معها شيء من خصوص الافعال كان المقدر بحسب المعنى فعلاً خاصاً كما اذا قلت: زيد على الفرس» او «من العلماء» او «في البصرة كان المقدر راكب و معدود ومقيم وذلك لا يخرجها عن كونها ظرفاً مستقراً لان معنى الفعل الخاص استقرفيها ايضاً وجاز تقدير الفعل العام فقط لتوجيه الاعراب فقط و لما كان تقدير الافعال العامة مطرداً ضابطاً اعتبره النحاة وفسروا المستقر بما متعلقه محذوف عام». انتهى. و ربما استفهم منه عدم الفرق بين القولين وكون النزاع لفظياً و الحق انه معنوى، ضرورة انه لا يمكن تقدير العام فى نحو قولهم: من لك بالمهذب و من لى بالمصدق» بل لابد من تقدير خاص اى: من يضمن، اللهم الا ان يكون مراده ان تفسير هم المستقر بذلك تفسير للكلى بصفة اغلب افراده فان ثبت هذا (ای: ما نسبه اليهم) ثبت ذلك (اى: عدم الفرق بين القولين و الا فلا فتأمل.
Like in the second, i.e. just as in the second it settles as will come, and the second is settled, because the pronoun of its attached element settles in it. For in, for example, 'Zayd is in the house,' when the attached element is deleted, its pronoun transfers to 'in the house' and settles in it, so it is 'that in which it settles' [mustaqarr fih], but they say 'al-mustaqarr' just as they say 'al-mushtarak' for 'al-mushtarak fih'. Or because the meaning of its deleted factor is understood from it, so it is as if it settles in it. Al-mustaqarr in this sense is also of the category of deletion of the relative clause. That said, it has been said: The zarf mustaqir is that whose factor is from the general verbs that no verb is devoid of, such as kawn, wujud, husul, and others that indicate absolute existence, while laghw is that whose attached element is from the specific verbs that indicate a specific mode of existence, whether deleted or not. And the verifying scholars are on the first [view]. Al-Sharif said in his hashiya on al-Kashshaf: 'Zarf mustaqir according to them is that whose attached element is not mentioned and is understood from it, so that the attached element is settled in it. If nothing is understood from it except general verbs, then the estimated [factor] is from them; if alongside them something specific is understood, then the estimated [factor] is a specific verb according to meaning, as when you say: Zayd is on the horse, or from the scholars, or in Basra—the estimated are: riding, counted, and residing. That does not remove it from being a mustaqir because the meaning of the specific verb settles in it as well, and it is permissible to estimate only the general verb for merely directing the i'rab. And since estimating the general verbs is regular and systematic, the grammarians adopted it and defined mustaqir as that whose attached element is a deleted general [verb].' End. It might be inferred from this that there is no difference between the two sayings and that the dispute is verbal, but the truth is that it is substantive, necessarily because it is not possible to estimate the general [verb] in sayings like: 'Who can guarantee for me the refined one and who can guarantee for me the truthful one?' but rather a specific [verb] must be estimated, i.e. 'who guarantees'. Unless his intention is that their interpretation of mustaqir in that way is a description of the universal by the quality of most of its individuals, so if this (i.e. what he attributed to them) is established, then that (i.e. the lack of difference) is established, otherwise not. So reflect.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines two types of zarf (adverb/prepositional phrase) in Arabic grammar: mustaqir (settled) and laghw (trivial). The author first explains the etymology of mustaqir as derived from 'mustaqarr fih' (that in which something settles), alluding to the deletion of the muta'allaq (attached element). Then two definitions are presented: (1) The first definition from earlier context: mustaqir is when the pronoun of the deleted muta'allaq transfers to the zarf and settles in it. (2) The second, more widely accepted definition: mustaqir is a zarf whose factor is a general verb like 'to be' (kawn), while laghw is a zarf whose muta'allaq is a specific verb like 'to ride' (rukub). The verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) uphold the first definition (the one based on deletion of muta'allaq). Al-Sharif al-Jurjani is then quoted from his hashiya on al-Kashshaf, elaborating that in mustaqir, the muta'allaq is deleted but understood. He gives examples: 'Zayd is on the horse' implies the specific verb 'riding', but the general verb 'to be' can be estimated for grammatical analysis because it is systematic. The author then addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think the two definitions reduce to a mere verbal dispute. He refutes this by giving an example where a general verb cannot be estimated: 'Man laka bi-l-muhadhdhab' (Who can guarantee for me the refined one?) requires a specific verb like 'yadman' (guarantees). Thus the difference is substantive, not merely verbal. The lesson highlights that grammatical definitions have practical consequences in interpreting constructions.

Key Terms
الظرف المستقر
الظرف اللغو
المتعلق
فعل عام
فعل خاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second definition in the passage, what distinguishes zarf mustaqir from zarf laghw?
A) The number of words in the phrase
B) The type of verb (general vs specific) that governs it
C) Whether the adverb is deleted or not
D) The presence of a pronoun in the adverb

Key Takeaway
The distinction between mustaqir and laghw hinges on whether the adverbial's governing verb is a general verb of existence or a specific verb of action, and this difference has real consequences in parsing and interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defining Ghayat: Cut-Off and Built Adverbs
Author defines ghayat as adverbs cut off from idafa and built, lists them, and cites a disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ghayat adverbs in Arabic text and verify the two defining conditions (cut off from idafa and built) and the heard list limitations.

Core Idea
Ghayat are adverbs that become 'goals' by taking the place of a deleted noun.

In Arabic grammar, a special class of adverbs called 'al-ghayat' (goals) is defined by being cut off from idafa and becoming built (mabniyya). This lesson unpacks that definition, explores why they are named 'goals', and notes the authoritative list of words accepted through sam' (heard usage). The commentary also records an extension debate, showing how grammatical categories are carefully delimited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'al-ghayat' in the Arabic. Note that the author begins by stating: 'Al-ghayat are the adverbs that are cut off from idafa and built.' The definiendum is a class of adverbs.
2
Extract the defining properties
The definition gives two essential properties: (1) cut off from idafa (qaṭ' 'an al-iḍāfa) – meaning the adverb is separated from its genitive complement; (2) built (mabniyya) – meaning it is invariable in case. These together constitute the differentia.
3
Understand the naming rationale
The author explains that originally these adverbs do not express goals; rather, the true goal is the deleted noun (mudaf ilayhi). After deletion, the adverb takes on the meaning of that noun and becomes a goal itself, hence the name 'ghayat'.
The name highlights the unusual semantic shift.
4
Identify the heard list and the no-analogy rule
The author lists specific words heard from the Arabs: qabl, ba'd, taht, fawq, amam, qadam, wara', khalf, asfal, dun, awwal, min 'ali, min 'ulu. Synonyms like yamin and shimal are explicitly excluded because they are not part of the transmitted set.
This shows the principle of sam' (audition) over qiyas (analogy) in this category.
5
Analyze the extension debate
Some scholars argue that ghayat should apply to all six directions in all states, because each direction necessarily indicates a limit. The author presents this as a minority view, inviting reflection.
This probes the boundaries of the definition.

في مقدمة الكتاب . (۸۶) الغايات هى الظروف التي قطعت عن الاضافة و بنيت وانما سميت غايات لانه كان حقها ان لا تكون غايات لتضمنها المعنى النسى بل تكون الغايات هى المنسوب اليه فلما حذف المنسوب اليه وضمنت هى معناه استغرب صيرورتها غاية المخالفة ذلك لوصفها فسمى بذلك الاسم لاستغرابه و تم كل و بعض مقطوعى الاضافة غايتين الحصول التعويض عن المضاف اليه. كذا ذكره نجم الائمة». وقيل : لانها قد تذكر بعد ذكر مصداق ما تضاف اليه فتأمل . وقال بعض المحققين: لا يبعدان يطلق الغايات على الجهات الست في جميع أحوالها لان كلامنها يدل على غاية من غايات الشيء التزاماً كما لا يخفى. ثم اعلم ان المسموع من ظروف الغايات «قبل» و «بعد» و «تحت» و «فوق» و «امام» و «قدام» و «وراء» و «خلف» و «اسفل» و «دون» و «اول» و من عل و من علو ولا يقاس عليها ما هو بمعناها نحو «یمین» و «شمال» و «آخر» وغير ذلك. صرح بذلك جماعة منهم الرضى (ره) (محمد على) وقال صاحب التقريب: قوله و بعد هو من الغايات الى الظروف التي قطعت عما تضاف اليه الذي هو الغاية حقاً فان قولنا جئت بعد زيد مثلاً معناه حد يجيني مجيء زید قبلی و جنت قبل ،زید ای حد مجیثی مجی زید بعدى فلما حذف المضاف اليه الذي هو الغاية اقيمت قبل» و «بعد» و اخواتها مقامه وسميت باسمه فقيل لها غاية. التقريب ص ١٠) (۸۷) ای لـ «بعد لا للغايات فان الغايات لا تطلق الا على الظروف المقطوعة المبنية فحينئذ لها حالة واحدة لاغيره اللهم الا ان يراد بالغايات الظروف المذكورة مطلقا اما على سبيل التجوز أو بناء على اطلاق الغايات عليها في جميع الحالات كما نقلناه عن بعض المحققين فتأمل . (محمد علی)
In the introduction of the book. (86) Al-ghayat are the adverbs that are cut off from idafa and built (mabniyya). They are called ghayat because it was appropriate for them not to be ghayat since they contain the meaning of 'nisy' (forgetfulness/relative?), rather the goals are the ones to which they are attributed. When the attributed-to is deleted and they themselves assume its meaning, their becoming a goal becomes strange, contrary to their description, so they were given that name due to its strangeness. And 'all' and 'some' of those cut off from idafa become two goals to obtain compensation for the deleted madaf ilayhi. Thus mentioned by Najm al-A'imma. And it was said: because they are mentioned after mentioning the instance of what they are added to, so reflect. And some verifying scholars said: It is not far-fetched that ghayat be applied to the six directions in all their states, because each of them indicates purpose (ghaya) from among the purposes of a thing necessarily, as is not hidden. Then know that what has been heard (sam' ) from among the ghayat adverbs are: qabl, ba'd, taht, fawq, amam, qadam, wara', khalf, asfal, dun, awwal, min 'ali, min 'ulu. And what is synonymous with them is not to be measured by analogy, like yamin, shimal, akhir, and others. A group including al-Radi (R) explicitly stated this. (Muhammad Ali) And the author of al-Taqrib said: His saying 'wa ba'd' is from the ghayat to the adverbs that are cut off from what they are added to, which is the true goal. For our saying 'I came after Zayd' for example means: the limit of my coming is Zayd's coming before me, and 'I came before Zayd' means: the limit of my coming is Zayd's coming after me. When the madaf ilayhi which is the goal is deleted, 'qabl' and 'ba'd' and their sisters take its place and are named by its name, so they are called ghaya. (al-Taqrib p. 10) (87) i.e. for 'ba'd' not for the ghayat, for ghayat are only applied to the cut-off, built adverbs; then they have one state and nothing else. Unless ghayat is intended for the mentioned adverbs absolutely, either metaphorically or based on applying ghayat to them in all states, as we transmitted from some verifying scholars. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-ghayat' (the goals) as a class of Arabic adverbs that are cut off from idafa (genitive construction) and built (mabniyya, invariable). The author explains the rationale for the name: originally, these words (like 'before', 'after') should not be goals themselves; rather, the true goal is the noun they are added to (e.g., in 'before Zayd', the goal is Zayd's time). When that noun is deleted, these adverbs take on the meaning of the goal, and this unusual role gives them the name 'ghayat' (goals). A quote from Najm al-A'imma is given, along with an alternative view that they are called ghayat because they are mentioned after the actual instance of the goal. Another scholar opines that the term can be extended to all six directions (perhaps front/back, up/down, right/left) in all contexts, since each direction implies a limit of the object. The passage then lists the specific adverbs that have been heard (sam' ) from the Arabs as ghayat: 'before', 'after', 'under', 'above', 'in front', 'in front (qadam)', 'behind', 'behind (khalf)', 'lower', 'without', 'first', 'from above', 'from on high'. Notably, synonyms like 'yamin' (right) and 'shimal' (left) are not included by analogy; only the heard set is accepted. The author then quotes from 'Sahib al-Taqrib' who illustrates with the example of 'jitu ba'da Zayd' (I came after Zayd), explaining that the deleted madaf ilayhi (Zayd's coming) is the true goal, and the adverb takes its place. A brief clarification follows: the term 'ghayat' properly refers only to the built, cut-off adverbs, not to those in normal idafa (like 'qabl' in 'qabl Zayd' which is not cut off). The hashya ends with an invitation to reflect, as some scholars extend the term to all states. This lesson establishes a precise grammatical category distinct from regular spatial or temporal adverbs.

Ghayat are a restricted set of adverbs that are cut off from idafa and built, and only those transmitted through Arabic usage are accepted.

Understanding Ghayat: Adverbs Cut Off from Idafa
Author defines 'ghayat' and lists the specific adverbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grammatical class of 'ghayat' and list the specific Arabic adverbs that qualify as 'ghayat'.

Core Idea
Ghayat are adverbs that become fixed (mabni) after their true referent is deleted.

In Arabic grammar, certain adverbs undergo a transformation when their attached noun (mudaf ilayh) is omitted. These adverbs become 'ghayat' — they are built (mabni) and take on the meaning of the goal. This lesson unpacks the definition provided in the text and gives you the exact list of these adverbs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key transformation
Recognize that ghayat are originally adverbs in idafa, but the mudaf ilayh is deleted and the adverb itself incorporates its meaning, becoming mabni.
2
Identify the true ghaya
The deleted mudaf ilayh is the real goal; the adverb replaces it grammatically.
For example, in 'ji'tu ba'da Zaydin', Zayd is the true goal.
3
Learn the list of approved adverbs
The text specifies which adverbs are heard as ghayat: qablu, ba'du, tahtu, fawqu, amamu, qiddamu, wara'a, khalafu, asfalu, duna, awwalu, min 'alu, min 'alwu. Note that synonyms like yamin are not included.
The list is restricted to what is heard (masmu') and cannot be extended by analogy.
4
Apply the concept
Practice with the example 'ji'tu ba'da Zayd': the phrase implies a limit (Zayd's coming) and with deletion, 'ba'du' becomes the ghaya.
This illustrates the transformation from idafa to ghaya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغايات هى الظروف التي قطعت عن الاضافة و بنيت وانما سميت غايات لانه كان حقها ان لا تكون غايات لتضمنها المعنى النسى بل تكون الغايات هى المنسوب اليه فلما حذف المنسوب اليه وضمنت هى معناه استغرب صيرورتها غاية المخالفة ذلك لوصفها فسمى بذلك الاسم لاستغرابه و تم كل و بعض مقطوعى الاضافة غايتين الحصول التعويض عن المضاف اليه. كذا ذكره نجم الائمة». وقيل : لانها قد تذكر بعد ذكر مصداق ما تضاف اليه فتأمل . وقال بعض المحققين: لا يبعدان يطلق الغايات على الجهات الست في جميع أحوالها لان كلامنها يدل على غاية من غايات الشيء التزاماً كما لا يخفى. ثم اعلم ان المسموع من ظروف الغايات «قبل» و «بعد» و «تحت» و «فوق» و «امام» و «قدام» و «وراء» و «خلف» و «اسفل» و «دون» و «اول» و من عل و من علو ولا يقاس عليها ما هو بمعناها نحو «یمین» و «شمال» و «آخر» وغير ذلك. صرح بذلك جماعة منهم الرضى (ره) (محمد على) وقال صاحب التقريب: قوله و بعد هو من الغايات الى الظروف التي قطعت عما تضاف اليه الذي هو الغاية حقاً فان قولنا جئت بعد زيد مثلاً معناه حد يجيني مجيء زید قبلی و جنت قبل ،زید ای حد مجیثی مجی زید بعدى فلما حذف المضاف اليه الذي هو الغاية اقيمت قبل» و «بعد» و اخواتها مقامه وسميت باسمه فقيل لها غاية. التقريب ص ١٠)
The 'ghayat' are the adverbs that have been cut off from idafa and are built (mabni). They are called 'ghayat' because it was originally their right not to be 'ghayat' (ends) since they contain the meaning of 'nisiyy' (forgotten), but rather the 'ghayat' (ends) are the thing to which they are attributed. So when the 'mansub ilayh' (the thing to which they are attributed) is deleted, and they (the adverbs) are made to contain its meaning, their becoming the end (ghaya) is strange, contrary to their description, so they are named with that name due to its strangeness. And 'tamma' and 'ba'd' are two of the adverbs cut off from idafa to obtain compensation for the mudaf ilayh. This is mentioned by Najm al-A'imma. And it was said: because they may be mentioned after mentioning an instance of what they are added to, so reflect. And some of the verified scholars said: It is not far that 'ghayat' be applied to the six directions in all their states, because each of them indicates a limit (ghaya) of the limits of the thing implicitly, as is not hidden. Then know that what is heard from the adverbs of 'ghayat' are: 'qablu', 'ba'du', 'tahtu', 'fawqu', 'amamu', 'qiddamu', 'wara'a', 'khalafu', 'asfalu', 'duna', 'awwalu', 'min 'alu', 'min 'alwu'. And what is synonymous with them like 'yamin', 'shimal', 'akhar', etc. is not to be analogized to them. This is explicitly stated by a group including al-Radi (may Allah have mercy on him). And the author of al-Taqrib said: His saying 'wa ba'du' is of the 'ghayat' to the adverbs that are cut off from what they are added to, which is truly the 'ghaya'. For example, our statement 'ji'tu ba'da Zaydin' means: the limit of my coming is Zayd's coming before me, and 'ji'tu qabla Zaydin' means: the limit of my coming is Zayd's coming after me. So when the mudaf ilayh which is the 'ghaya' is deleted, 'qablu' and 'ba'du' and their counterparts are put in its place and are named by its name, so they are called 'ghaya'.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ghayat' (singular ghaya) as applied to certain Arabic adverbs. The author explains that these adverbs are originally attributed to a mudaf ilayh, but when the mudaf ilayh is deleted and the adverb itself takes on the meaning of the goal, they become grammatically 'mabni' (built) and are called 'ghayat' because of the oddity of their function. The passage then gives the specific list of adverbs that qualify: 'qablu', 'ba'du', 'tahtu', etc., and explicitly excludes words like 'yamin' and 'shimal' which are synonyms but not used in the same way. A quote from Sahib al-Taqrib provides an example: 'ji'tu ba'da Zaydin' means the limit of my coming is Zayd's coming before me, so the deleted mudaf ilayh (Zayd) is the true 'ghaya', and 'ba'da' is placed in its position. This is a definitional lesson clarifying a grammatical concept central to Arabic syntax, which is also relevant to logical analysis of propositions involving temporal/spatial relations.

Key Takeaway
Ghayat are a specific set of Arabic adverbs that are cut off from idafa and become mabni, taking the meaning of the deleted object.

Definition and Restriction of 'Ghayat' in Arabic Grammar
Author specifies that 'ghayat' applies only to cut-off built adverbs, not to all uses of 'ba'du'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which Arabic adverbs qualify as 'ghayat' and explain the restriction applied by the glossator.

Core Idea
The term 'ghayat' applies only to cut-off built adverbs, not to every use of 'ba'du'.

The glossator Muhammad 'Ali refines a potentially misleading statement that 'ba'du' is from the 'ghayat'. He clarifies that 'ghayat' is a technical term limited to specific adverbs that are cut off from idafa and indeclinable. This precision prevents confusion between the category and the word's general usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base statement
Recall that the commentator stated 'wa ba'du huwa min al-ghayat'. This seems to imply that 'ba'du' belongs to the category of 'ghayat'.
This is the starting point for the glossator's refinement.
2
Extract the glossator's restriction
The glossator says 'al-ghayat la tutilaqu illa 'ala al-zuruf al-maqtu'at al-mabniyah'. This restricts the term 'ghayat' to cut-off built adverbs only.
'Maqtu'at' means cut off from idafa; 'mabniyah' means indeclinable.
3
Apply the restriction to 'ba'du'
Consequently, 'ba'du' is a ghaya only when it meets that condition (cut-off and built). When it is used in idafa (e.g., ba'da dhalika), it is not a ghaya.
This resolves the apparent contradiction in the base statement.
4
Consider the exception
The glossator notes that some scholars apply 'ghayat' to these adverbs in all their usages, either metaphorically or as a general term. This is a separate opinion.
The glossator leaves it to the reader to reflect ('ta'ammal').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۷) ای لـ «بعد لا للغايات فان الغايات لا تطلق الا على الظروف المقطوعة المبنية فحينئذ لها حالة واحدة لاغيره اللهم الا ان يراد بالغايات الظروف المذكورة مطلقا اما على سبيل التجوز أو بناء على اطلاق الغايات عليها في جميع الحالات كما نقلناه عن بعض المحققين فتأمل . (محمد علی)
It means for 'ba'du' and not for the 'ghayat', for the 'ghayat' are not applied except to the cut-off built adverbs. Then in that case they have one state and no other, unless by 'ghayat' what is meant is the mentioned adverbs absolutely, either metaphorically or based on applying the term 'ghayat' to them in all states, as we transmitted from some verified scholars, so reflect.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This short note clarifies a potential confusion. The commentator had said 'wa ba'du huwa min al-ghayat', which might be interpreted as 'ba'du' is part of the ghayat category. The glossator (Muhammad 'Ali) points out that actually 'ba'du' is one of the ghayat, but the term 'ghayat' itself is reserved for those specific cut-off built adverbs. So when we say 'ba'du' is a ghaya, we mean it belongs to that set, not that the word 'ba'du' in all its uses is called a ghaya. The ghayat have a single state (mabni) unlike other adverbs that can be declinable. However, some scholars allow calling the mentioned adverbs as ghayat in all their uses, either metaphorically or as a general usage, but that is a separate view.

The Third Case: Adverb Built Due to Intended Deleted Mudaf Ilayh
Author explains the third state where the adverb is built

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third case where an Arabic adverb (zarf) is built (mabni) and explain the role of the intended deleted mudaf ilayh (manwiyy).

Core Idea
The adverb is built when the deleted mudaf ilayh is intended, mirroring a particle's dependency.

In Arabic grammar, adverbs like 'qablu' and 'ba'du' can be either declinable (mu'rab) or indeclinable (mabni) depending on the presence and intention of their mudaf ilayh. This lesson focuses on the third case, where the mudaf ilayh is omitted but mentally intended, causing the adverb to become built because it needs that deleted element, similar to a particle needing its object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
Recall the three states: (1) mudaf ilayh present → adverb declinable; (2) mudaf ilayh deleted and forgotten (nasiyyan mansiyyan) → adverb built but without need; (3) mudaf ilayh deleted but intended (manwiyy) → adverb built due to need.
The passage contrasts the third case with the first two.
2
Confirm the deleted element is intended
In the third case, the speaker intends a specific mudaf ilayh but does not mention it. For example, 'kuntu qablu' could mean 'I was before [something specific]' where the specific event is intended but not stated.
This is different from the second case where 'kuntu qablu' means simply 'formerly' with no specific reference.
3
Analyze the resemblance to particles
The adverb now resembles a particle (harf) because both require a complement (the deleted mudaf ilayh for the adverb, the object for the particle). This need causes the adverb to be built.
Particles are always built; the analogy explains the buildup.
4
Contrast with the second case
In the second case, the deleted element is completely forgotten (nasiyyan mansiyyan), so there is no need for it. The adverb is still built, but the reason is different: it is not an idafa construction.
The passage explicitly says 'lam yakun muhtajan ilayhi' (there is no need for it) in the second case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۹) قوله و على الثالث مبنية : اى على تقدير ان لا يذكر معها المضاف اليه وكان منوياً. المشابهتها الحرف في الاحتياج الى ذلك المحذوف بخلاف الحالة الثانية فانها لما كان المحذوف فيها نسياً منسياً غير مراد للمتكلم بها لم يكن محتاجاً اليه لتكون هذه الظروف مبنية للاحتياج اليه فمعنى كنت قبلاً اى قديماً وكنت بعداً اى آخراً و هكذا من غير ملاحظة شيء يعتبر التقدم والتأخر بالنسبة اليه و بخلاف الحالة الاولى فانه وان كان الاحتياج حاصلاً لها مع وجود المضاف اليه ايضاً لكن ظهور الاضافة فيها يرجح جانب الاسمية لاختصاصها بالاسماء. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'and in the third case it is built (mabniyah)' – i.e., on the assumption that the mudaf ilayh is not mentioned with it and is intended (manwiyy). Its resemblance to the particle (harf) in needing that deleted element, unlike the second case, because when the deleted element in it is nasiyyan mansiyyan, not intended by the speaker, it does not need it, so these adverbs are built due to the need for it. Thus the meaning of 'kuntu qablu' is 'formerly' and 'kuntu ba'du' is 'latterly', and so on, without considering anything in relation to which the before and after are considered. And unlike the first case, because even though need exists for them along with the existence of the mudaf ilayh, the appearance of idafa in them gives weight to the nominal side because idafa is specific to nouns.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This note explains the third grammatical state of these adverbs, where the mudaf ilayh is deleted but mentally intended (manwiyy). In this case, the adverb is built (mabni) because it resembles a particle (harf) in needing a complement. This is contrasted with the second case where the deleted element is completely neglected (nasiyyan mansiyyan), so there is no need, and the adverb is also built but for a different reason? Actually, the text says in the second case the adverb is not built because there is no need? Wait: it says 'bikhilaf al-hala al-thaniya fainnaha ... lam yakun muhtajan ilayhi litakuna hadhihi al-zuruf mabniyatan lil-ihiyaj ilayhi'. The phrasing is complex. It seems that in the second case, since the deleted is not intended, there is no need, so the adverbs are not built? Or they are built? Actually, the text says 'lam yakan muhtajan ilayhi litakuna...' possibly meaning that they are not built? But the classification is: first case (mudaf ilayh present) -> adverb is declinable (mu'rab) due to idafa; second case (deleted and forgotten) -> adverb is built (mabni) but with no need? The gloss clarifies that in the third case the adverb is built because of the need for the intended deleted element. The second case is different because the deleted is not intended, so the adverb is built without need? The commentary should reflect the explanation.

Key Terms
المبنية
المنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the mudaf ilayh is present.
B) Because it resembles a particle in needing the deleted intended element.
C) Because the mudaf ilayh is forgotten.
D) Because it is a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
The adverb is built in the third case because the deleted mudaf ilayh is intended, creating a need similar to a particle's dependency, unlike the second case where no such need exists.

The Linguistic Definition of Iman as Tasdiq
Argues that Iman linguistically means heartfelt belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic evidence for Iman as heartfelt belief and apply the principle that religious terms retain their linguistic meaning without evidence of transference.

Core Idea
Iman is fundamentally heartfelt belief, not mere speech.

Before we can distinguish Islam from Iman, we must understand what Iman truly means. The author argues that the core of Iman is 'tasdiq' (heartfelt belief), based on Quranic usage and Arab custom. He also introduces a key principle: religious terms should keep their linguistic meaning unless there is clear evidence of a shift. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find where the author states that Iman means heartfelt belief (tasdiq qalbi).
The claim is supported by Shaykh al-Ta'ifa.
2
Extract linguistic evidence
Look at the Quranic verses and Arab usage cited: 'wa ma anta bi mu'min lana' and 'yu'minu bi al-jibti'. Notice how 'iman' is used to mean 'believing' in a heart sense.
Arabs say 'fulan yu'minu bi kadha' meaning 'he believes in such-and-such'.
3
Understand the principle of no transference
The author asserts 'al-asl 'adam al-naql' – the default is that words keep their original meaning. Only if there is proof of a new meaning (naql) can we abandon the linguistic meaning.
This principle is important for interpreting all Shari'i terms.
4
Refute the counterargument
Some argue that belief cannot be known without speech, so speech must be part of Iman. The author refutes this with the example of a mute person, whose belief is still valid despite inability to speak.
This shows that belief is independent of speech.
5
Conclude the essence of Iman
The author concludes: 'fa al-haqq anna haqiqat al-iman huwa al-tasdiq al-qalbi'. Verbal confession and actions are necessary for its manifestation, not its essence.
This sets up the distinction from Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بمقتضاه ام لا فهو اعم مطلقا من الايمان كما يدل عليه موثقة سماعة قال : قلت لابي عبدالله عليه السلام اخبرني عن الاسلام والإيمان أهما مختلفان ؟ فقال (ع): «ان الايمان يشارك الاسلام و الاسلام لا يشارك الايمان فقلت صفهمالي فقال : الاسلام شهادة أن لا اله الا الله والتصديق برسول الله (ص) به حقنت الدماء وعليه جرت المناكح و المواريث و على ظاهره جماعة الناس والايمان الهدى و ما ثبت في القلوب من صفة الاسلام وما ظهر من العمل والايمان ارفع من الاسلام بدرجة ان الايمان يشارك الاسلام فى الظاهر و الاسلام لا يشارك الايمان في الباطن و ان اجتمعا في القول و الصفة و غير ذلك من الاخبار و قوله تعالى: قالت الاعراب آمنا قل لم تؤمنوا و لكن قولوا اسلمنا و لما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم ، صريح في ذلك الفرق. فمن قال بالترادف بينهما فلا ينبغي الالتفات اليه. و اما قوله تعالى: (إن الدين عند الله الاسلام فلا دلالة الآية المذكورة و قوله
His statement: 'and if it is an expression for the totality of verbal confession', know that Islam on this [view] would be synonymous with Iman, for its meaning is also that, as narrated from al-Rida (peace be upon him and his family). And what is understood from the statement of Shaykh al-Ta'ifa is that Iman is more general than Islam, for he said: 'The meaning of Iman is heartfelt belief and consideration of what flows on the tongue.' I say: one can argue for that because Iman in language means heartfelt belief, as Allah Almighty said narrating from the brothers of Yusuf (AS): 'wa ma anta bi mu'min lana...' i.e., you are not believing our words, and He said: 'yu'minu bi al-jibti wa al-taghut' i.e., he believes. And the Arabs say in their conversations: 'fulan yu'minu bi kadha wa fulan la yu'minu bi kadha.' And the principle is the absence of transference [of meaning], i.e., no transference of the meaning of tasdiq to another meaning, and there is no evidence for it, i.e., for the transference. And every religious term for which there is no evidence of transference must be carried on its linguistic meaning. And what is said that heartfelt belief is not understood by custom without speech, so speech must be considered in the concept of Iman, is questionable because heartfelt belief may be understood without it, as in the case of the mute and the silent. So the truth is that the reality of Iman is heartfelt belief, and consideration of the tongue and other limbs is necessary for its manifestation. (Abd al-Rahim) And (Mirza Muhammad Ali, may Allah have mercy on him, in some hashya) said: Know that Islam, as understood from narrations, is verbal confession that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad (SAW) is the Messenger of Allah, whether with heartfelt belief and action accordingly or not. So it is absolutely more general than Iman, as indicated by the reliable tradition of Sama'a: he said, I said to Abu Abdullah (AS): inform me about Islam and Iman, are they different? He said: 'Indeed, Iman shares with Islam, but Islam does not share with Iman.' I said, describe them to me. He said: 'Islam is the testimony that there is no god but Allah and belief in the Messenger of Allah (SAW); by it blood is protected, by it marriages and inheritances proceed, and outwardly the people are upon it. And Iman is guidance and what is established in the hearts from the description of Islam and what appears of action. Iman is higher than Islam by a degree. Iman shares with Islam in the outward, and Islam does not share with Iman in the inward, even if they combine in speech and description.' And other such narrations. And the saying of Allah Almighty: 'The bedouins said we believe; say you do not believe, but say we have submitted, for faith has not yet entered your hearts' is clear regarding that difference. So whoever says they are synonymous should not be paid attention to. As for His saying: 'Indeed the religion with Allah is Islam,' there is no indication in it, for the general term is used for each of the individuals. So Islam is of two types: one is without Iman, that is mere verbal confession and acknowledgment, and the second is with verbal confession, belief in the heart, and action accordingly with the limbs, and from this is the mentioned verse and His saying...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a theological and linguistic argument distinguishing Islam from Iman. The author begins by noting that if Islam is defined as 'the totality of verbal confession', it becomes synonymous with Iman (faith). However, he argues against this synonymy. He cites the view of Shaykh al-Ta'ifa (al-Tusi) that Iman is more general, being heartfelt belief. Then he presents a linguistic argument: the root meaning of Iman in Arabic is 'tasdiq' (belief/assent), as seen in Quranic usage. He defends that religious terms should retain their linguistic meaning unless there is evidence of transference. He refutes the objection that belief cannot be known without speech, citing the case of a mute person. He concludes that Iman is essentially belief in the heart, with verbal confession and actions being necessary outward manifestations. Then he presents a second view from Mirza Muhammad Ali: based on narrations, Islam is verbal confession, which is absolutely more general than Iman, because one can be a Muslim without inner belief. He adduces the hadith of Sama'a and the Quranic verse about the bedouins to show that you can be Muslim (outwardly submit) without being a believer (inwardly). The verse 'The religion with Allah is Islam' does not prove synonymy because 'Islam' is used generically for both categories: mere submission and submission with belief. This is a classic theological discussion with direct implications for logic and definition: terms must be defined precisely, and homonymy must be recognized.

الإيمان
التصديق
النقل

A) It should be taken as a technical term with a new meaning defined by scholars.
B) It should be understood according to its original linguistic meaning.
C) It should be ignored until evidence appears.
D) It should be interpreted in a metaphorical sense.

Key Takeaway
Iman is essentially heartfelt belief; outward expressions are manifestations, not the essence.

Distinguishing Islam from Iman: Outward Submission vs. Inner Belief
Shows that Islam is verbal confession, Iman is inner belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Islam (outward submission) and Iman (inner belief) using the hadith and Quranic evidence provided.

Core Idea
Islam is outward submission; Iman is inner belief; they are not synonymous.

Now that we have established that Iman is inner belief, we turn to its relationship with Islam. The author presents a second view based on narrations: Islam is defined as verbal confession of the shahada, which can exist without inner belief. This makes Islam absolutely more general than Iman. We will examine the key hadith from Sama'a and the Quranic verse about bedouins that support this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of Islam from the hadith
In the hadith of Sama'a, Imam al-Sadiq (AS) defines Islam as 'shahada an la ilaha illa Allah wa al-tasdiq bi rasulillah' – verbal testimony and belief in the messenger. Note that this definition does not require heartfelt belief; it is about outward confession.
The hadith says: 'bi-hi huqinat al-dima' – by it blood is protected, i.e., legal rulings apply outwardly.
2
Understand the relational statement
The Imam says: 'al-iman yusharik al-islam wa al-islam la yusharik al-iman'. This means that whenever there is Iman, Islam is also present, but Islam can exist without Iman. Iman is more specific; Islam is more general.
This is a key logical relation: Iman is a subset of Islam.
3
Interpret the Quranic verse about bedouins
Allah says: 'qalat al-a'rab amanna qul lam tu'minu wa lakin qulu aslamna'. The bedouins said 'we have believed,' but Allah commands to say 'we have submitted' instead, indicating that outward submission (Islam) is not the same as inner faith (Iman).
This verse is cited as decisive evidence for the distinction.
4
Note the two types of Islam
The author explains that 'Islam' in the Quranic verse 'Inna al-din 'inda Allah al-islam' does not prove synonymy. Rather, 'Islam' is a general term that includes both the mere outward submission and the submission accompanied by faith and works.
Thus, the verse can be applied to both categories.
5
Apply the distinction practically
The hadith explicitly says that by Islam (shahada) blood is protected, marriages and inheritances are valid. A person who merely pronounces the shahada is treated as a Muslim in outward rulings, even if his belief is questionable. However, true Iman (inner belief) brings additional degrees of reward and is required for salvation in the afterlife.
This has direct implications in fiqh (e.g., funeral prayers, inheritance).

Conventional vs Linguistic Meaning of 'Idāfah'
Commentator contrasts the conventional and linguistic definitions of 'idāfah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the conventional and linguistic meanings of the term 'idāfah' and explain why logicians clarify both.

Core Idea
A technical term's meaning may differ from its everyday use; precise definition is key.

This brief note from a logical commentary draws attention to two levels of meaning for the term 'idāfah': its conventional (technical) meaning in logic and its linguistic (etymological) origin. Understanding this distinction helps you read logical texts with precision and avoid confusing everyday language with technical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions given
Read the Arabic: 'هذا معناه العرفي و معناه اللغوى رباط القربة'. The commentator explicitly states the conventional meaning (العرفي) and then the linguistic meaning (اللغوي).
2
Note the conventional meaning
The conventional meaning is 'connection' (الرباط). In logic, 'idāfah' often refers to a relation or genitive construction.
This is the technical sense used in defining logical terms.
3
Note the linguistic meaning
The linguistic meaning is 'tying a water skin' (رباط القربة), illustrated by the phrase 'he tied the water skin with the nose band'.
This concrete action is the root from which the abstract term is derived.
4
Understand the relationship
The conventional meaning is derived metaphorically from the linguistic meaning: just as a water skin is bound, so terms are 'bound' in a genitive construction.
5
Apply the distinction
When you encounter 'idāfah' in a logic text, you must determine whether the author intends the technical sense (connection) or a reference to the etymology. Usually it is the technical sense.
This skill prevents misinterpretation of technical passages.

The example 'he tied the water skin with the nose band' illustrates the original concrete meaning. This is a typical lexicographical clarification found in logical commentaries to ensure precise understanding of technical terms.

Defining Saj‘ and Distinguishing it from Qāfiyah
Commentator defines saj‘ and refines its comparison to qāfiyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the terminological definition of saj‘, distinguish it from qāfiyah, and explain why it is not applied to the Qur’an.

Core Idea
Saj‘ is the agreement of prose clauses on one final letter, not the cooing of a dove or poetic rhyme.

Precise terminology is vital in Islamic logic and rhetoric. This passage from the commentator offers a clear definition of saj‘ (rhymed prose) while carefully distinguishing it from qāfiyah (poetic rhyme). It also addresses a common misapplication regarding the Qur’an. Understanding these nuances sharpens your analytical tools for textual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Find the two definitions
Locate the linguistic meaning (cooing of a dove) and the terminological definition (agreement of two prose clauses on one final letter). Note that the technical term is what matters for rhetoric.
2
Compare saj‘ with qāfiyah
The commentator notes that some equate saj‘ in prose with qāfiyah in poetry, but he refines this: qāfiyah is not merely agreement on a final letter; it has its own technical conditions. Identify what the passage says makes qāfiyah different.
The key is that qāfiyah is more than just matching letters; it is a specific structure in poetry.
3
Identify the requirement for saj‘
Saj‘ requires a pause (waqf) and building on a consonant (sukūn). Without these, the pairing of clauses (muzāwaja) does not occur. Extract this condition from the text.
The phrase 'الاسباع مبنية على سكون الاعجاز' summarizes this.
4
Understand the Quranic exception
The passage explicitly states that 'saj‘' is not used for the Qur’an; instead, 'fawāṣil' is used. Find the reason given: because saj‘ linguistically originates from dove cooing, and it is deemed inappropriate for divine speech.
This shows the sensitivity to linguistic origins in Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٢٦) ای كما انه لقصد الحصر، فانه لا مانع من ان يكون لشىء اسباب متعددة وجهات متشتتة . فانت بمرئى من سعاد و مسمع». ثم السجع في اللغة هدير الحمام و نحوها قال الشاعر: حمامة جرعى حومة الجندل اسجعي و في الاصطلاح «هو تواطؤ الفاصلتين من النثر على حرف واحد في الآخر، وقد يقال على الكلمة الاخيرة من الفقرة باعتبار كونها موافقة للكلمة الاخيرة من الفقرة الاخرى». و هذا مراد من قال ان السجع في النثر كالقافية في الشعر لا المعنى الأول فان القافية لا تطلق على تواطؤ الكلمتين من أواخر الابيات على حرف واحد بل هو مقابل للتقفية. ثم المراد بالسجع ان يزاوج بين الفواصل ولا يتم ذلك في كل صورة الا بالوقف والبناء على السكون. ولهذا قالوا: الاسباع مبنية على سكون الاعجاز قيل: ولا يقال في القرآن اسجاع لان السجع في الاصل هدير الحمام و نحوها بل يقال فواصل . (محمد على)
(126) i.e., as it is for the purpose of limitation, for there is no impediment to a thing having multiple causes and dispersed aspects. 'You are in my sight, O Su‘ad, and in my hearing.' Then al-saj‘ linguistically is the cooing of a dove and the like. The poet said: 'O dove of the meadow of Hūmat al-Jandal, coo.' And terminologically: 'It is the agreement of the two clauses of prose on one final letter, and it may be said of the last word of a paragraph considering it as agreeing with the last word of another paragraph.' This is what is meant by one who says that saj‘ in prose is like qāfiyah in poetry – not the first meaning, for qāfiyah is not applied to the agreement of two words at the ends of verses on one letter, but rather it is the opposite of taqfiyah. Then the intended meaning of saj‘ is to pair the pauses, and that is not achieved in every form except by stopping and building on a consonant. For this reason they said: 'Saj‘s are built on the silence of the endings.' It is said: 'And saj‘ is not said in the Quran because saj‘ originally is the cooing of a dove and the like; rather it is said: fawāṣil.' (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines 'saj‘' (rhymed prose) both linguistically and terminologically. Linguistically, it means the cooing of a dove. Terminologically, it is the agreement of two prose clauses on the same final letter. The commentator distinguishes it from 'qāfiyah' (rhyme in poetry): qāfiyah is not simply the agreement of two words on one letter, but a specific structure in poetry. Saj‘ is about pairing pauses (fawāṣil), and it requires stopping (waqf) and building on a consonant (sukūn). The commentator notes that the term 'saj‘' is not applied to the Quran; instead, the divisions are called 'fawāṣil' (breaks). This distinction is important for logic because rhymed prose is a tool in rhetoric, one of the five logical arts, and precise terminology is crucial.

القافية
الفاصلة

A) The cooing of a dove

C) The agreement of two prose clauses on one final letter
D) The final word of a prose paragraph

Key Takeaway
Saj‘ is the agreement of two prose clauses on one final letter, distinct from qāfiyah, and not applied to the Quran, which uses fawāṣil instead.

?
Take a well-known hadith, e.g., 'إِنَّمَا الْأَعْمَالُ بِالنِّيَّاتِ'. Check if it contains saj‘ by seeing if the two clauses (إِنَّمَا الْأَعْمَالُ and بِالنِّيَّاتِ) agree on one final letter. Explain your reasoning using the terminological definition.

Definite and Indefinite in Logical Definitions
Commentator explains definiteness choices in logical terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definite article is used or omitted in a logical definition based on prior mention and identify the role of the covenant article.

Core Idea
Defining with 'al' requires prior mention; otherwise, the indefinite form is used.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks why the author introduced the two sections (logic and kalam) with the definite article while leaving the introduction indefinite. The key lies in the grammatical concept of the covenant article (al-'ahd), which requires prior mention. Understanding this helps you read Islamic logical texts with precision, noticing how definiteness signals familiarity or prior reference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two occurrences of definiteness
The passage contrasts the definiteness of the two sections (القسمين) with the indefiniteness of the introduction (المقدمة). Note that the commentator uses the term 'تعريف' for definiteness and 'تنكير' for indefiniteness.
In Arabic logic texts, the definite article often marks terms that have been previously introduced or are well-known in the discipline.
2
Understand the requirement for the covenant article
The commentator states that the definite article (al) of covenant attaches only when there is a requirement (مقتض), and here that requirement is prior mention (تقدم ذكرهما). For the introduction, no prior mention exists, so it remains indefinite.
The covenant article here is 'al-'ahd', referring to a specific previously mentioned entity, not generic definiteness.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The commentator anticipates an objection: absence of the covenant article does not imply absence of all definiteness, because definiteness is not limited to that article. He then notes a refined objection: the external covenant can indicate a specific instance even without prior mention. The response implies that in this context, the author's use is based on the specific textual history.
This shows the layered argumentation typical of Islamic logical commentaries.
4
Relate to the introductory grammatical point on 'lammā'
The passage begins by explaining the word 'lammā' as either a causal particle or a temporal adverb. This is relevant because the phrase 'since it was known implicitly' (لما علم ضمنا) introduces the rationale for the definiteness choices.
The commentator's analysis of 'lammā' shows how grammatical precision supports logical exposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لما علم ضمناً: كلمة «لما» اما ظرف او حرف بمعنى «اد» ترد لربط مضمون جملة بوجود مضمون جملة اخرى كما اذا قلنا : «لما جاء زيد جاء عمرو» فمعناه عند القائل بالحرفية : ان وجود الاول سبب لوجود الثانى وعند القائل بالظرفية : ان الثانى وجد عند وجود الأول سواء كان بالسببية او بالاتفاق. ثم الغرض من هذا الكلام بيان سبب تنكير المقدمة وتعريف القسمين و حاصله ان التعريف اى ((ال) التعرف شيء على الكلمة لا يرتكب اليه الا لمقتض و المقتضى هيهنا بالنسبة الى القسمين موجود و هو تقدم ذكرهما فلذا عرف القسم الأول اى ادخله «ال») و اما بالنسبة الى المقدمة فلا ولذا نكرها فلايرد انه لا يلزم من انتفاء العهد انتفاء التعريف، لعدم الحصاره انحصاره فيه، لكن لكن يرد ان العهد الخارجى هو الاشارة الى حصة معينة و ان لم يتقدم ذكرها لا لفظاً ولا دلالة. ثم انما قدم المصنف القسم الاول لانه مقدمة موصلة الى القسم الثانى (شيخ عبدالر.
His statement: 'since it was known implicitly': The word 'lammā' is either a zarf or a harf meaning 'when', used to connect the content of one sentence with the existence of the content of another, as when we say: 'When Zaid came, Amr came.' Its meaning according to the one who says it is a harf: that the existence of the first is the cause of the existence of the second. According to the one who says it is a zarf: that the second occurred at the occurrence of the first, whether by causality or coincidence. Then the purpose of this statement is to explain the reason for the indefiniteness of the introduction and the definiteness of the two sections. The summary is that the definite article (al) does not attach to a word except for a requirement. Here the requirement with respect to the two sections exists — that is, they were previously mentioned — so he made the first section definite (i.e., he entered 'al' onto it). As for the introduction, there is no such requirement, so he left it indefinite. So it cannot be objected that the absence of the definite article of covenant necessarily implies the absence of definiteness altogether, because definiteness is not confined to it. However, it can be objected that the external covenant is the indication of a specific instance even if it was not previously mentioned, either verbally or by implication. Then the author presented the first section first because it is a preamble leading to the second section.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'since it was known implicitly' (لما علم ضمنا) from the matn. The commentator first explains the grammatical nature of the word 'lammā', noting two views: it is either a particle (harf) indicating causality, or an adverb (zarf) indicating time. He then clarifies the main point: why the author introduced the first section with the definite article and left the introduction indefinite. The reason is that the definite article of covenant requires a prior mention. Since the two sections (logic and kalam) had been mentioned before, they are defined; the introduction was not mentioned, so it remains indefinite. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: one might think that absence of the covenant article implies no definiteness at all. He replies that definiteness is not restricted to that article. Another objection follows: the external covenant (العهد الخارجي) can refer to a specific instance even without prior mention. He points out that the author's reason is based on the specific context. Finally, he notes that the first section is presented first because it serves as a preamble to the second.

Two Meanings of Tawakkul: Mystical vs. Linguistic
Commentator distinguishes between mystical and linguistic meanings of tawakkul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the mystical meaning of tawakkul from its linguistic meaning, and explain how the hadith 'tie your camel' illustrates the correct Islamic understanding.

Core Idea
Tawakkul is trust with effort, not passive resignation.

The term 'tawakkul' (reliance on Allah) is often misunderstood as passivity. In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the true, mystical meaning is not mere linguistic reliance but includes taking necessary means while entrusting the outcome to Allah. The prophetic command to tie one's camel before trusting Allah serves as the perfect example. Understanding this distinction is essential for both spiritual practice and correct theological belief.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions given
Read the text and locate where the commentator states the mystical meaning ('ترك السعى فيا لا يسعه قدرة البشر') and the linguistic meaning ('الاعتداد بالشيء'). Note the key difference: the mystical meaning adds the condition of abandoning what is beyond human capacity, while the linguistic meaning simply means reliance.
The mystical meaning is supported by a hadith, indicating it is the religiously significant sense.
2
Analyze the hadith example
The hadith 'اعقل بعيرك وتوكل على الله' is presented as evidence for the mystical meaning. Break it down: the command to tie the camel represents taking the means (the cause), while trusting Allah represents not attributing the result to the means. This shows that tawakkul does not negate action but negates relying on the action alone.
The hadith is a key proof-text in debates about tawakkul and kasb (acquisition).
3
Contrast with the linguistic meaning
The commentator explicitly says 'هذا معناه العرفانى لا اللغوى'. The linguistic meaning is simply 'reliance on a thing' without any condition of abandoning effort. Understand that the technical term in tasawwuf has a more specific connotation than the everyday Arabic usage.
This distinction guards against the error of equating tawakkul with fatalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل هو ترك السعى فيا لا يسعه قدرة البشر فيأتى بالسبب ولا يحسب ان المسبب منه و عليه الحديث: «اعقل بعيرك وتوكل على الله» . (محمد علی) (۱۲۸) هذا معناه العرفانى لا اللغوى وهو فى اللغة: الاعتداد بالشيء، يقال: توكل عليه ای به و اعتمد عليه . (التقريب ص ۱۳)
And it is said: It is abandoning striving in what is not within the capacity of man, so he brings the cause and does not think that the caused is from it. And upon this is the hadith: 'Tie your camel and rely on Allah.' (Muhammad Ali) (128) This is its mystical meaning, not the linguistic one. And it is in language: reliance on a thing. It is said: tawakkala 'alayhi, meaning he relied upon it and depended on it. (al-Taqrib, p. 13)
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'tawakkul' and distinguishes two meanings. The commentator first reports a definition: tawakkul is to abandon striving in what is beyond human power, to take the means but not attribute the result to oneself. This is supported by the prophetic hadith commanding both action (tie your camel) and trust (rely on Allah). Then the commentator clarifies that this is the mystical (irfani) meaning, not the linguistic one. In the Arabic language, tawakkul simply means reliance or dependence on something. The distinction is important because the technical term in tasawwuf carries additional connotations of giving up one's own effort in matters where human agency is insufficient, while the linguistic meaning does not necessarily exclude effort. This guards against the misunderstanding that tawakkul in the religious sense means passivity.

Key Takeaway
The mystical meaning of tawakkul combines taking means with trusting Allah, unlike the broader linguistic meaning of mere reliance.
